Source: Somalia Statistical Abstract, 1968.
About 2 million ha of arable land in the inter-river area (at present largely
bush covered) have sufficient rainfall (400-600 mm) to allow dry land farming.
In lower rainfall areas full or partial irrigation would be required for cul-
tivation. Irrigation development is centered on the two major rivers, the
Juba and the Shebeli; other streams are seasonal torrents. This irrigation
has been undertaken both on a more commercial basis (by banana and sugarcane
producers) and on primitive basis in the case of many small-scale mostly fruit
and vegetable producers along the river banks.
4.        All land is owned by the Government and although some land regis-
tration mostly for non agriculture purposes (sites for houses etc.) was prev-
iously undertaken in the Northern region, agricultural land in particular is
held largely by traditional right of usage. The vast rangelands are used by
the nomadic herdsmen, and although the exact territory is sometimes difficult
to ascertain, particular areas are retained exclusively by specific tribal
groups.
5.        The rights to cultivable land and water 1/ are more clearly defined.
Cultivable land, whether or not actually cultivated, is used by specific clan
groups and in some cases is divided among clan sections. All aatural and Gov-
erpment constructed water facilities are for common usage. Wells or pans dug
by individuals belong exclusively to those individuals, but no one may be
refused water for himself or his family. The owner of the well may, however,
refuse water for livestock.
1/   Used for people and animals.  There are no laws or traditions that
regulate water usage for irrigation.



ANNEX 1
Page 3
A. Agriculture
Irrigated Crops
6.        Bananas were for many years the predominant export crop and Somalia's
largest single source of foreign exchange (Table 2). They still corntribute
approximately 26% (1971) of export earnings. Production of sugar, the most
important commercial crop for domestic consumption, is under the control of
the Societa Nationale Agricule Industriale (SNAI). Sugarcane is grown in
the irrigated areas (with water drawn from the Shebeli river) of the Jowhar
region. Government is considering the extension of the sugarcane industry
into the Juba river region, and establishment of sugarcane plantations is
now being examined. Potential exists for the production of citrus, rice,
cotton and groundnuts. Plans for establishment of state farms for produc-
tion of these commodities, particularly in the Juba river region, are either
in the process of implementation or being examined.
Dry-Land Crops
7.        Maize millets and sorghum are all predominately subsistence crops,
and any small surpluses are sold on local markets. Yields are normally low;
extensive losses are generally the result of both adverse weather conditions
and damage from insects, birds, and other pests. Recent research work at Afgoi
has demonstrated that local crop varieties have excellent production poten-
tial, and improvements in fertilizer distribution, coupled with Government
initiative in marketing and favorable weather have resulted over the last
two years in surplus production. Surpluses are at present purchased and
stored by Government.
8.        Cotton is grown throughout Somalia, but the industry is beset with
problems of seed supply, pest and disease control, inadequate extension and
marketing services, high ginning costs, and poor organization. Yields are
low but there appears to be no inherent technical reasons why cotton pro-
duction should not be further developed.
9.        Oilseeds include mainly sesame and groundnuts, with some safflower,
sunflower, soya, and coconut. Some are used for edible oil purposes (sesame,
groundnut, coconut) in a number of small oil expressing plants. Cottonseed
is likely to become increasingly important in the future.
B. Livestock Sub-Sector
10.       Most of Somalia's land area is suitable only for extensive live-
stock production, and the majority of the people are traditional pastoralists
who are not only wholly dependent on livestock, but who have long experience
of the difficulties of handling stock under the often harsh conditions im-
posed by sparse grazing and water supplies. Estimates of total livestock
population are 2-1/2 to 3 million cattle, about the same number of camels,
and about 12 million sheep and goats.



ANNA 1
Page 4
11.       The most pressing needs in the sub-sector are for measures to re-
lieve present constraints on increased production and offtake by improving
facilities for marketing and disease control and the introduction of cattle
fattening operations. Livestock fattening would in particular open up new
export markets in chilled and frozen meat for the livestock industry which
is of special importance in view of the limited scope for the expansion of
live animal exports.
12.       60% of the estimated cattle population are found in the Southern
part of Somalia, south and west of Mogadishu. The four main breeds, the
Boran, Jiddu, Ghave and Abgal are all derived from the Zebu type (Bos Indicus).
The Ghave and Abgal breeds are used mainly for milk production. The Jiddu
afid Boran are dual purpose animals, used for milk and meat production as
well as for draft purposes. The Boran is well known in East Africa, and is
found in Kenya and Ethiopia as well as Somalia.
13.       Somali cattle are hardy and well adapted to the generally harsh
environment and without further genetic improvement, fertility and growth
rates of beef cattle would respond to improved disease control and nutri-
tion.  Further genetic upgrading is necessary however to improve milk pro-
duction.
14.       Little is known of herd age and sex composition, but observation
and discussion with producers indicate a high percentage of female stock.
This is due to a number of factors: first, the environment demands that in
difficult times breeding stock get preference for food and water; second,
the social prestige attached to large cattle numbers; third, the lack of mar-
ket outlets for young immature cattle; and fourth, the nutritional needs of
the family, which among nomadic pastoralists includes a large proportion of
meat. Thus, when cattle are killed for human consumption young male calves
are slaughtered first, followed by older males and aged cows. Such are the
vagaries of the climate that often all but the most valuable female breeders
must be sold, killed, or simply allowed to die. No reliable figures are
available but the cattle offtake is probably about 9 to 10% (see Annex 2).
Sheep and Goats
15.       There are 4 million small fat-tailed, black-faced sheep, useful
for milk and meat production but of no value for wool. Eight million goats
make use of otherwise virtually worthless rangeland, and are a valuable source
of meat, skins, contributing to export revenues. Weaning percentages in sheep
and goats are about 50% p.a. and losses from disease and parasites are very
high (in excess of 20% p.a.). Annual sales and slaughterings total approx-
imately 4 million head of sheep and goats an offtake of some 30%.
Camels
16.       The camel is the most common mark of individual wealth among the
pastoralists and is afforded priority in respect of grazing, water and man-
agement. Camels are valuable beasts of burden, and supply the bulk of sub-
sistence milk requirements. Some are also slaughtered or exported live for
meat, and the total offtake rate is estimated at 5%.



ANNEX 1
Page 5
Disease Control
17.       Disease control is a continuing, though lessening constraint on im-
proved productivity in the livestock sub-sector. Government now supplies in-
dividual livestock owners with all veterinary drugs and vaccines free of charge.
This program, provided it is reinforced by expanded extension services, should
result in increased producer acceptance of animal health control measures.
18.       Until recently Rinderpest was the principal disease affecting cat-
tle. Vaccination of all cattle is now compulsory and an eradication and mass
vaccination program should greatly reduce the incidence of the disease.
This program is concurrently being conducted by Kenya, Ethiopia and
Somalia, and is financially and technically assisted by the Federal Re-
public of Germany.
19.       Foot and mouth disease is endemic, and although sporadic outbreaks
occur, it is not a serious threat to indigenous cattle under present grazing
conditions. It is, however, a serious constraint to the export of livestock
and meat, particularly to the more lucrative European markets.
20.       Trypanosomiasis is a major problem in the Juba valley area, caus-
ing considerable losses to stock owners every year, particularly in the dry
season. At this time livestock which have been widely dispersed during the
rains concentrate in the riverine areas which are widely infested with tsetse
fly. Other diseases affecting the herds are Contagious Bovine Pleuropneumonia
and Contagious Caprine Pleuropneumonia (which causes heavy losses in the goat
herds and for which no vaccine has been produced). Various tick-borne diseases
are also present; Anaplasmosis and Heartwater often pose a threat to cattle and
sheep. Anthrax and East Coast Fever are now known to exist in some areas.
Constraints to Improved Livestock Production
21.       Major constraints to increased livestock production are summarized
below:
(a) Inadequate nutrition in the form of both feed and water, par-
ticularly in the drought years which occur about 2 years in
five;
(b) Incidence of disease and parasite infestation affecting fer-
tility, growth rates, and mortality;
(c) Lack of reliable and properly distributed animal (and human)
watering facilities over the arid rangelands, resulting in
heavy livestock concentrations and severe rangeland over-
grazing near pernanent water points during dry periods;
(d) Inadequate marketing facilities affecting all classes and
ages of livestock;



ANNEX 1
Page 6
(e) Absence of a specific market for young immature animals,
resulting in substantial slaughtering of very young mostly
male calves and an economic loss in uncontrolled exports of
potential feeder cattle to Kenya; and
(f) The absence of any fattening operation, the provision of which
would in turn generate a demand for feeder or immature cattle
and offer an opportunity for cheap growth on ranches and in-
tensive finishing in feedlots.
C. Training, Extension Services and Research
Training
22.       Training in the Ministry of Livestock, Forestry and Range (MLFR)
is concentrated upon the Training School for Animal Health Assistance (TSARA)
in Mogadishu, assisted by the United Nations Development Program. The TSAHA
provides a 2 year training course of Diploma standard in elementary animal
health control, animal husbandry and management, and range improvement and
management. The course includes a reasonable proportion of practical and
field experience, and diplomates are generally well-regarded. The school
has a capacity of some 80 students, and about 60 graduates are now employed
in the Department of Animal Health and Production of the MLFR. A recent in-
novation at the TSAHAhas been the introduction of short "in service" train-
ing courses for those who previously graduated or who joined the Department
field staff without prior training.  The School also undertakes adaptive re-
search in range improvement and animal production and a small feedlot was
recently established to provide information on beef cattle fattening in
Somalia based on feeding molasses, bagasse, silage, hay and concentrates.
Extension Services
23.       Agricultural extension services are poorly developed and confined
mainly to the activities of the Department of Animal Health and Production
within the MLFR. Veterinary services (Annex 19) are provided by some 400
veterinary field staff, including about 60 TSAHA graduates and foreign-
trained technicians. Extension is largely confined to vaccination and
disease treatment. Veterinary centers are under-equipped and for the most
part lack adequate transport facilities - both of which impede effective
operation.
24.       The increase in available trained staff has led to the launching
of the "Extended Programme of Intensification of Animal Health", which is
aimed primarily at disease control and the eventual establishment of a disease
free zone in the Lower Juba Area. The concept of concentrating limited re-
sources in the area with greatest potential for improved livestock produc-
tion is basically sound. In conjunction with the Rinderpest eradica-
tion program, good progress is being made. The proposed Trans-Juba Livestock
Project would provide further assistance for this animal health control Pro-
gram.



ANNEX 1
Page 7
25.       Diagnostic services are provided at the TSAHA in Mogadishu, and at
a center established at Hargeisa which is staffed with three qualified Somali
veterinarians. Within the Department, vaccine production is carried out by
the Serum and Vaccine Institute in Mogadishu.
Research
26.       Adaptive research is being conducted at the TSAHA and the Serum and
Vaccine Institute in Mogadishu. Further work is being conducted in connec-
tion with dairy cattle, crops, pastures and irrigation at the "21st October
Dairy Farm" at Afgoi operated by the Livestock Development Agency (LDA) (Annex
3). Livestock improvement programs, including management and adaptive research
are being conducted with cattle breeding at Hargeisa and sheep breeding and
range management at Asura. Agricultural and horticultural research using irri-
gation and a wide variety of fodder, grain and agricultural crops (including
citrus, papaya and cotton) is conducted at the Afgoi Research Station, which
was initially established with USAID assistance. However, that research gen-
erally lacks the direction and understanding necessary for a sustained ef-
fort; these, coupled with a dedicated extension service, are essential if
effective results are to be achieved.
27.       In 1970 a UNDP project was initiated in the Northern region.  This
was designed to survey and classify range lands, initiate selective bush con-
trol techniques, determine sociological patterns, investigate water resources,
and develop acceptable range management techniques. Plans are now in hand
for the extension of this program to other parts of the country.

SOMALIA
TRANS-JUBA LIVESTOCK PROJECT
Markets and Prices
General
1.        Somalia has a well established traditional livestock marketing
system. Most towns or villages have a cattle market where buyers (traders,
butchers) and sellers (stock-breeders, farmers, nomads) gather daily. Sales
are generally carried out through middlemen or brokers, who are paid from
2-3 percent of the sale price by both buyer and seller. In the Juba Region,
auction sales are also popular. The auctioneer, or "delal", sells the
animals to the highest bidder. Payment is generally made at the town
council offices, where the animals are registered together with the price
and the names of the parties concerned. In more remote areas, traders use
trucks or camels to visit the nomads. Slaughter cattle are purchased
either for cash or in exchange for consumer goods, but the prices paid
nomads in barter deals are lower than those paid at the markets. Breeding
stock sales are informal and independent of the market place. These are
general barter sales in exchange for other stock.
Cattle Population and Offtake
2.        Since there are no accurate figures for livestock population in
Somalia, available estimates vary widely. The usually quoted figure of
2-1/2 million cattle is probably conservative, as this estimate is outdated,
and there has been a marked increase in herd size over the past two years
(Annex 1, para. 10). This increase has led to overstocking in some areas
and serious range deterioration. About 10% of Somalia's cattle migrate
seasonally into Kenya and Ethiopia. In 1972 total offtake was estimated to
be about 250,000 head, or over 9%; domestic consumption accounted for about
110,000 head, live exports 80,000 head and exports of processed meat another
60,000 head.
3.        According to estimates obtained during the Rinderpest vaccination
campaign, there are now approximately 700,000 and 850,000 head of cattle in
the Lower and Upper Juba areas respectively. The cattle markets and stock-
routes proposed under the Project would draw cattle from 80-85% of Lower
Juba area and 40-50% from the Upper Juba area - a total herd of between
900,000 and 1 million head. Present offtake in the Project area is esti-
mated at about 65,000; 30,000 head are purchased by LDA for sale to the
Kismayu meat factory or for live export, 5,000 head are processed in



ANNEX 2
Page 2
municipal slaughterhouses, about 30,000 head are sold overland into Kenya,
and a small nuwiber are moved toward the North for live export. With an
improved offtake rate of about 10%, some 90,000 head would thus be available
from the area. The estimated additional 53,000 head to be purchased by LDA
under the Project would be available from additional offtake and by the
reduction of sales to non-LDA buyers.
Marketing Infrastructure in the Trans-Juba Area
4.        Although cattle density is highest in the central part of the Lower
Juba region stretching from Kismayu to the southern edge of the Dujuma sub-
region, cattle are found practically everywhere in the Juba region with
the exception of tsetse fly infested river areas. The main flow of cattle
to Kismayu comes from east of the Juba River and includes some cattle from
the western bank and adjacent areas crossing over along the way. LDA's
buying activities in Hadweineh, Afmadu and Badada have in recent years
increased the number of cattle coming from the southern half of the Lower
Juba region.
5.        The climate necessitates seasonal movement of nomadic Somali herds
into Kenya and Ethiopia and the sale to Kenyan buyers has been a traditional
practice. Prices, paid in Kenya Shillings, are probably low but established
ties with traders, who also supply a range of consumer goods unavailable in
Somalia, and the dearth of alternative outlets in Somalia have sustained
this trade. The Kenya Meat Commission provides a ready market for canner
cattle and ranches and feedlots for store cattle. In the more remote parts
of the Lower Juba and throughout all of the Upper Juba region, suitable
buying points and market channels are lacking; it is therefore nearly
impossible to channel surplus animals through Kismayu for processing or live
export.
6.        With the completion of the Kismayu meat factory and Kismayu Port,
the demand for cattle for canning and live export has risen sharply in the
past few years, and LDA has set up permanent markets in Badada and Kismayu
to secure the required supply. A holding ground near Kismayu has been
partially developed and some improvements have been made to stockroutes.
The new marshalling yard near the Kismayu port has helped export of live
cattle while a new holding ground near Gelib and a system of stockroutes
east of the Juba river, established with EEC assistance, are expected to
improve the flow of cattle from that region (Annex 3).
7.        The marketing facilities in the Trans-Juba area are inadequate,
however, even at the present demand for cattle: the Badada-Kismayu road is
not serviceable for 6-8 months of the year; the unfinished Kismayu holding
ground is too small to handle present LDA purchases; there are not enough
staging points and watering facilities along the existing stockroutes, which
cover only a relatively small part of the area between the Kenya border and
the Juba river. As a result, mortality on the stockroutes is presently



ANNEX 2
Page 3
estimated at 1.5%, while weight losses of arriving cattle are nearly 13%.
With the Project, mortality of cattle for canning and live export would be
expected to decline to 1% and weight losses to 7.5%. Weight losses for
cattle destined for the two grazing ranches or the feedlot of the Irrigated
Fodder Farm would be less than 7% due to shorter walking distances (Table 1).
Finished cattle would be delivered to the meat factory by truck.
Prices
8.        Prices paid to producers vary widely, both geographically, seasonal-
ly and according to market destination. In the North where exports have been
regular and fairly well organized, prices have at times been twice as high
as in the South. But a slackening in export sales invariably depresses
prices to producers in all parts of the country. Furthermore, forced sales
in dry seasons produce gluts on the markets, and coincidental with the
generally poor condition of the animals, bring about producer prices far
below the annual average.
9.        The Livestock Development Agency (LDA) (Annex 3) is a Government
agency engaged primarily in the purchasing of livestock for live export and
sale to the Kismayu meat factory (Annex 4). Its current purchase prices are
based on the following schedule but are varied to meet circumstances at the
time of purchase:
Cows for canning    Any weight         So.Sh.0.81 per kg liveweight
Bulls and steers    220-350 kg         So.Sh.0.81 - 0.88 per kg liveweight
Steers               350 kg and over  So.Sh.0.92 per kg liveweight
It is estimated that 40% of LDA purchases are now made directly from cattle
producers and 60% from middlemen. LDA purchases and sales for the period
1969-1972 are shown in Table 2. Projected sales resulting from the Project
are presented in Tables 3 and 4.  Table 5 provides details on the cattle
purchased for fattening.
10.       LDA is the sole supplier of slaughter cattle to the Kismayu meat
factory.  Negotiated fixed prices are currently So.Sh. 0.98 per kg liveweight
for canning animals with an average weight of 250 kg, and So.Sh. 1.60 for
cattle qualifying for freezing. Purchase and selling prices are at present
too low to guarantee a continued supply to the meat factory as well as for
live export, and LDA intends to increase them in 1973. It has been assummed
at appraisal, therefore, that buying prices from nomads for canning animals
would increase per kg liveweight to So.Sh. 0.87 from the present So.Sh. 0.81;
prices for steers for live export or the feedlot of the Irrigated Fodder Farm
would increase to So.Sh. 1.00 from the present So.Sh. 0.92 bought for live ex-
port. Furthermore, it has been agreed that the price of canners sold by LDA
to the Kismayu meat factory would inczease per kg liveweight from So.Sh. 0.98
to So.Sh. 1.20. The sale price for emport live cattle (So.Sh. 1.53 per kg
net of export charges) would practically not change, and fattened animals



ANNEX 2
Page 4
would be sold to the meat factory for So.Sh. 2.20 per kg 1/. A complete
list of buying and selling weights and prices for cattle is provided in
Table 6.
11.       As LDA would in future be buying cattle at much greater distances
from their destination than at present, it would have to absorb some of the
cost of the weight losses now incurred by private traders and producers
(even though total weight losses would be reduced, Table 1). To cover these
losses, purchase prices in remote markets would have to be somewhat lowered.
Such differential prices are prevalent in the private sector where middle-
men buying cattle at higher weights in the hinterland pay lower prices
than producers moving cattle down the stockroutes themselves would receive.
Marketing Taxes and Fees
12.       Although the type and level of charges vary, the following taxes
and fees are those generally effective:
Brokerage fee                             2-3% of value
Market fee                                So.Sh.5 per head
"Zariba" charge 2/                        So.Sh.2 per head
Drovers charges to quayside               So.Sh.5 per head
Municipality tax                          5% of value
Export charges:
Customs duty                            So.Sh.27 per head
Veterinary Certificate                  So.Sh.10 per head
Shorehandling and Stevedoring fees    So.Sh.9 per head
Harbor tax                              1.5% of value
13.       LDA has had a special arrangement with the Kismayu municipality,
and pays a fixed charge of only So.Sh.4 per head in taxes on the cattle it
exports instead of the 5% required by law. This amount is expected to be
increased to So.Sh.10 per head (and has been included in Project estimates).
There are no export taxes on processed meats, although the meat factory
pays a slaughter tax of So.Sh. 8.50 and a market tax of So.Sh. 3.00 per
head. Private traders collect a minimum fee of 15% of the value of the
sale. LDA's cost of marketing also includes mortality and weight losses
(para 11) and the cost of operating and maintaining infrastructure such
as stockroutes and holding grounds.
1/   ihis price is based on the 1973 Kenya Meat Commission prices and a 50%
killhiig out ratio which is less than yielded in Kenya.  These elements
are acceptable as guidelines as: animals to be fattened in Somalia are
nearly identical to those in Kenya; technology of fattening and type of
feed would be almost the same in both countries, and the chilled or
frozen beef produced from fattened animals in Somalia would be competitive
with similar type of meat from other countries on foreign markets.
2/   For feeding and other care while in transit.



ANNEX 2
Page 5
Domestic Meat Consumption
14.       Meat is an important part of the Somali diet, particularly among
the livestock-raising population, and ranks second only to milk. Camel and
goat meat are preferred. In 1971 municipal slaughterhouses slaughtered
about 440,000 sheep and goats, 105,000 cattle and 31,000 camels. Nationally,
municipal cattle slaughter increased by 25% over the preceding 2-year
period; in the Juba region, however, it decreased by 13%, largely as a
result of the Kismayu meat factory coming into full production. About
5,000 of the estimated 9,000 cattle slaughtered in municipal slaughter-
houses in the Juba region are assumed to have originated in the Project
area. It was estimated that in the Juba region cattle slaughter would
increase by about 1% annually, while regional slaughter, outside this
region, could rise at a faster rate. Incremental domestic meat consump-
tion would be mostly covered by sheep, goat and camel meat and by increased
local utilization of edible by-products from the Kismayu meat factory.
Exports of Livestock and Livestock Products
15.       Exports from the livestock sector have been increasing rapidly
in recent years, rising in value from So.Sh. 109.2 million in 1967 to
So.Sh. 162.4 in 1971. Over the same period, the share of processed meats
increased from 2% to 13% of livestock exports. Exports for the period
1965/71 shown in Annex 1 do not include unrecorded livestock exports from
small Somali ports and by land into Kenya.
16.       The majority of live animals are exported from the northern port
of Berbera because of the port's relative proximity to the importing states
such as Saudi Arabia, Kuwait, the Gulf States and, in earlier years, the
Arab Republic of Egypt. But despite the well established trade in live
animals, ocean shipping is poorly organized and losses are high due to
the use of unsuitable ships, insufficient provision for feed on board,
and irregular service at short notice. With the intention to alleviate
the problem, the Government recently established the Somali Shipping
Agency which will be responsible for improving ocean transport of livestock
and bananas. The Agency will probably acquire one or two cattle carrying
ships, but purchase plans and financing arrangements are not firm at this
time. Government has also decided to establish the Livestock Trading
Agency (LTA) which, when sufficiently organized and staffed, will assume
LDA's present responsibility for live animal exports. It is anticipated
that LTA would not only be involved in the commercial aspects of foreign
trade but would also organize and coordinate efficient transport to
importing countries.
17.       Demand for Somali livestock in Saudi Arabia, Kuwait and adjacent
Gulf countries has been increasing and is expected to remain strong. Trade
with the Arab Republic of Egypt, the largest potential market, has recently



ANNEX 2
Page 6
come to a standstill over a dispute concerning Somalia's preference for
payments made in hard currencies instead of the traditional barter arrange-
ments. In addition to the cattle sold overland to Kenya (and not in accordance
with Somali export regulations), a small but increasing number have also
been sold to Kenya through Kismayu Port. Live cattle exports from Somalia
increased from about 34,000 in 1969 to 81,000 in 1972. There is a strong
demand for Somali live cattle expecially in Saudi Arabia, Kuwait and in
other oil producing Gulf countries. Total numbers of live animals exported
through Kismayu Port are as follows:
Year        Cattle            Camels       Sheep/Goats
1966        2,260               -
1967        4,255               -
1968        1,854               -
1969        2,236               -
1970        3,022              402           1,801
1971        9,515            1,046           1,150
1972        5,567              687              -
18.       Until the opening of the Kismayu meat packing plant, the
privately owned small canning plant in Mogadishu and one in Merca, now
closed, were rhe only sources of processed meats for export. With the
Kismayu plant now in full production, the supply of processed meats available
for export has more than tripled. The USSR is the major importer of the
plant's present main product line, canned stewed steak, and has a long-
term contract for 16 million tins annually. At present Italy is also
importing a small quantity of processed (cooked) meat. Production of
corned beef at Kismayu is to be expanded. Export of chilled and frozen
beef, recently initiated at Kismayu, is expected to become one of the
factory's major activities, particularly in view of the expected
volume of feedlot - fattened steers and the completion in the near future
of a modern airport at Kismayu that would eventually enable air-transporta-
tion of meat. Higher quality chilled meat would be sold in Somalia's
traditional livestock markets such as Saudi Arabia, Kuwait and other oil
producing Gulf States, in Mediterrenean countries such as Libya, the Arab
Republic of Egypt, Lebanon, Cyprus, Greece, Italy, and in some African
countries e.g. Zaire. Veterinary standards in Somalia have been raised
to a level acceptable to those countries and no problems are anticipated
in exporting chilled or frozen beef.
19.       World prospects for processed meats in 1980 are estimated to
range from equilibrium in supply and demand to a sizeable supply deficit.
Prices would thus tend to remain stable or move upwards. However, as
Somalia is neither a major exporter of beef nor a supplier of the pre-
dominant import markets in Europe and North America, future prospects
for its exports are regionally determined. As indicated above, there



ANNEX 2
Page 7
is demand for live cattle and beef in countries within economic shipping
distance of Somalia. Total supply in regional markets will partly depend
on the ability of competitors to enter European markets. The additional
supply of 4,000 tons of chilled or frozen meat and 2,400 tons of canned
meat produced annually in Somalia (Annex 4, Table 3) will not be large
enough to affect prices. The additional proposed live export of 8,000
head of cattle by LDA is less than the average annual increase of 12,000
in the period 1969-1972, but larger increases are not likely because of the
limited availability of bulls and steers. A law prohibits the export of
females but this may have to be changed if a sufficient supply of animals
are to be made available for export. Government feels however that this
could be done only at a later stage when breeding animals would be pro-
tected from eventual export and slaughter. To this end, efforts must be
undertaken to reduce the slaughter of male calves.
20.       Somalia does not, as yet, have a disease free zone which would
enable the export of uncooked beef to European and other markets with
high sanitary requirements.  However, veterinary services now control
the spread of contageous diseases and guarantee selection of suitable
cattle for slaughter. Live cattle for export are quarantined on the
Kismayu holding ground using the following procedure:
(a) On reception, animals are held for 24 hours and then
inspected by the Veterinary Officer; he accepts or
rejects these after inspection for Trypanosomiasis,
Foot and Mouth Disease and Bovine Pleuropneumonia;
(b) Animals are marked or ear-tagged and sprayed, vaccinated
for Rinderpest, Anthrax, Foot and Mouth, and treated
against Trypanosomiasis;
(c) After being kept for 7 days in isolation paddocks the
cattle are moved to the grazing areas and retained for
a further 7 days. Twenty-four hours before release
animals are re-examined and a certificate issued.
Rejected animals fit for slaughter are sold to the meat
factory.
August 20, 1973

TRANS-JUBA LIVESTOCK PROJECT
Livestock Development Agency
1.        The Livestock Development Agency (LDA) was established by
Presidential Decree on February 16, 1966, as an autonomous Government
Agency. Its primary functions were to promote the marketing and export
of livestock and related products and to coordinate the development of
the livestock industry (Annex 19).
Livestock Marketing and Export Development
2.        In June 1967, the quarantine of animals before export became
compulsory. This necessitated the construction of marshalling yards and
quarantine stations at the three main ports of Berbera, Mogadishu and
Kismayu. LDA initially provided holding grounds and livestock marketing
facilities along the Shebeli and Juba rivers, particularly near Kismayu
and Mogadishu to hold export stock before entering the quarantine stations
and to collect and hold cattle destined for the Kismayu meat factory. In
Mogadishu, the Port Authority agreed to provide watering facilities and
also alloted land for the extension of the LDA constructed marshalling
yard. The Kismayu holding ground was partially completed in 1969, and
contained a cattle marketing facility and a race to brand, vaccinate and
spray the purchased stock against ecto-parasites. A second holding
ground was also established in Warmahan near Mogadishu. Two new holding
grounds designed to serve the northern port of Berbera are presently under
construction - one at Hargeisa and one at Burao. A second marshalling yard
at Berbara was equipped to hold about 2,000 cattle under cover for 14 days and
approximately 30,000 sheep and goats for a period of 48 hours. It had
standing facilities for about 1,000 camels for 48 hours before certifica-
tion for export. The third marshalling yard was completed at Kismayu and
an area of about 260 ha to serve as quarantine station. Early in 1967
LDA was given responsibility for all port veterinary work and the issue
of health certificates for animals and animal products exported from Somalia.
LDA now controls the export of live animals through all major and most of
the minor ports.
3.        In July 1966 LDA became responsible for the management and
development of the Serum and Vaccine institute in Merca and in 1967 it
received a Government grant of Sot Sh. 3.2 million (US$0.5 million) for the
construction of a new Serum and Vaccine Institute and a Hides, Skins and
Weather Development Center in Mogadishu which were completed at the end of
1969. Although the operational control of both projects has been given to



ANNEX 3
Page 2
the Directorate of Animal Health and Production in MLFR, financial responsi-
bility remained with LDA 1/. In 1967 LDA took over from the Government a
project for the establishment of 42 parasite treatment centers. A joint
parasite treatment program was negotiated with the British Consulting firm,
Cooper Mac Dougall, and FAO. It was not feasible to construct the 42 centers
originally proposed to be implemented in one year (1967) and a three year
program for the construction of 32 centers was therefore adopted. Only
three centers were ultimately established, two in 1967 and one in 1968. The
Veterinary Department has now taken over the operation of this program from
LDA.
4.        LDA started trading in drugs in 1969 and negotiated a loan of
So. Sh. 800,000 from the Somali Development Bank to develop this activity.
LDA's efforts to exempt drugs from import duties were unsuccessful. Due
to heavy import duties, high overhead charges, keen competition from the
private sector and finally the distribution of free drugs in the drought
stricken areas by the Veterinary Department, the sales, which had started
on an encouraging scale, slowed down and showed a heavy decline towards
the end of 1969. All trading in drugs ended early in 1972 when the
distribution of drugs free of charge became the responsibility of the
Veterinary Department.
LDA's Trading Activities
5.        In 1969 LDA became mainly engaged in the supply of slaughter
cattle to the Kismayu meat factory and in export of livestock. LDA was
supposed to sell slaughter cattle to the factory at the rate of 2,000
animals per month, but at that time the factory was able to process only
about 250. As a result of this limitation of capacity, combined with a
severe drought, increased weight losses both during cattle movement and
holding, LDA suffered considerable financial losses. In 1969 LDA sold only
about 1,600 animals to the factory, and about 1,000 for export. To avoid
further financial losses, LDA at that time began buying animals for the
factory's account using factory funds advanced to LDA. It charged a
2-1/2% commission on the purchase price plus an overhead charge of So. Sh. 53
(US$8.5) per head to cover purchase, quarantine and holding expenses.
The factory is now operating at almost full capacity and LDA is buying
cattle for its own account and reselling them to the factory at an agreed
price. Sales to the meat factory in 1972 totalled about 53,000 head, and
these are expected to rise to 60,000 in 1973 - the factory's full
processing capacity. LDA's livestock trading operations showed in 1969
a net loss of approximately So. Sh. 0.5 million (US$80,000), however in 1970
a net profit of about So. Sh. 0.5 million (US$80,000) and in each 1971 and
1972 So. Sh. 1.0 million (US$160,000).
1/   LDA continues to pay the Government counterpart contribution out of
its own earnings.



ANNEX 3
Page 3
Other Activities
6.        As part of the livestock improvement program, LDA took over the
management of the cattle breeding farm at Gedkedbleh, the poultry farm at
Aoorein and the sheep breeding center at Asura. The cattle and sheep
breeding facilities were experimental in nature and are still in operation.
The poultryfarm, however, proved unsuccessful and was closed down. Two new
breeding ranches for sheep and goats are provided for under the 1974-78
five-year plan. In August 1971 the Government took over the Afgoi Dairy
Farm established under the German Technical Assistance Program and handed
it over to LDA. Provisions were made to develop this dairy farm as a
supplier of milk to the Mogadishu Milk Factory.
Accounts
7.        LDA particularly suffers from a shortage of qualified and
experienced staff in the accounts section. LDA's records have been kept
basically on a cash receipts and payments basis. For organizational purposes
the country was initially divided into zones serving the major ports.
Some form of distinction was made between capital and revenue expenditure
in the early stages and in 1967 and 1968 attempts were made at preparing
balance sheets showing the assets and liabilities of the Agency; these
efforts were later abandoned, however. An attempt, to prepare a balance
sheet for LDA was again made in 1970, but since no records were kept of
capital expenditures, these were omitted except for an insignificant amount
of about So. Sh. 100,000 (US$16,000). The inadequacy of LDA's accounts
staff resulted in a lack of accurate records and of any form of management
information. Furthermore, inconsistent allocations of expenses makes a
meaningful comparison of the Income Statements and the results of the
different activities for the years 1966-1972 extremely time consuming and
to a large degree misleading. Moreover, LDA does not have an internal audit
section and hitherto no external audit has been performed on its accounts
(Table 1).
Financial Position
8.        Government initially began by allocating to LDA So.Sh. 1 million
in 1966 and contributed a further So.Sh. 3 million over the following six
years, part of which was to cover the expenses of the Serum and Vaccine
Institute and the Hides and Skins Center (para. 3).
9.        In January 1967, the Government transferred to LDA the right to
collect dues for port veterinary work, and these now provide a sizeable
part of LDA's income. In 1967, collections for port veterinary work amounted
to approximately So. Sh. 2.0 million (US$320,000) and these averaged
So. Sh. 3.0 million (US$480,000) over the following four years. In 1972,
income from this source exceeded So. Sh. 4.0 million (US$650,000).



ANNEX 3
Page 4
10.       During appraisal LDA's staff prepared a balance sheet as at
December 31, 1972 based on available information and the balances outstanding
on that date - some of which were arrived at by extrapolation (Annex 20, Table 2).
The- item "Fixed Assets" represents capital expenditure during 1972 only. The
item "Accounts Receivable" represents mainly amounts due from the only meat
factory of So. Sh. 1.2 million plus advances to agents of LDA of So. Sh. 3.1
million for the purchase of cattle. In addition the balance of "Cash on
Hand and at Banks' amounted to So. Sh. 1.9 million 1/, thus bringing the total
liquid cash on that date to So. Sh. 5.0 million (US$800,000).
11.       LDA's long-term debt is made up of three loans from the Somali
Development Bank totalling So. Sh. 3.2 million (US$510,000): So. Sh. 2.2
million was for the construction and development of the Warmahan holding
ground near Mogadishu, So. Sh. 800,000 was to assist LDA on developing its
drug trading activity (which ceased in 1971), and So. Sh. 200,000 was for
fodder trading (which did not develop as anticipated); all loans will be
completely repaid by 1975.
LDA's Financial Projections Outside the Project
12.       LDA's projections for the period 1973 to 1980 were based on the
assumption that: (a) inspection fees would remain constant; (b) the number
of inspected animals would increase annually by 1.5% for sheep and goats,
2% for cattle; (c) the sale of livestock to the meat factory would remain
constant at 60,000 head per annum, the present processing capacity of the
meat plant; and (d) the total of exported live animals would increase at
the rate of 5% per annum.
13.       In 1973, the Government introduced a turnover tax (Law No. 58
of July 31, 1972) based on sales and services rendered by public enterprises
and agencies, the percentage of which will be determined by the Secretary
of State for Finance for each fiscal year. The law stipulates that the
profits of an enterprise should be appropriated for the following purposes
in sequential order: (a) for the planned increase in the working capital;
(b) for setting up a personnel reward or incentive fund; (c) for setting
up a social and housing facilities fund; (d) for financing of non-centralized
capital investments; and (e) the remainder should be transferred to the
State budget. Certain provisions of the law are at present not clear and
it is also not known what percentage would be applied in assessing the
turnover tax or in the allocation of profits. Since all profits of
Government agencies would revert to the State, the turnover tax was, for
practical purposes, ignored in arriving at LDA's projected net profits.
LDA's projected cash flow was prepared as far as practicable along the
procedures prescribed, assuming an allocation of 5% and 7% of net profits
to the incentive fund and housing fund respectively (Table 2).
1/   LDA requires large sums of cash to carry out its cattle trading
activity which is necessitated by the long distances without adequate
communications and the lack of banking facilities in remote areas.



ANNEX 3
Page 5
14.       The same law also stipulates that each organization would create
a depreciation fund, a part of which (presently undetermined) would be
transferred to the Government Development Fund and the balance would be
used by the enterprise for major repairs of fixed assets and financing
of non-centralized capital investments. The amounts appropriated to this
fund appear in the balance sheet net of any amounts utilized for replacement
of fixed assets.
15.       LDA's projected cash flow shows that without the proposed Project
it would continue to have a net income of approximately So. Sh. 2.0 million
a year (US$320,000). Its cash generation would be sufficient (a) to service
its long-term debt; (b) to invest about So. Sh. 6 million (US$960,000) over
a period of five years in the development of two new sheep and goat farms
and the expansion of its livestock development schemes; (c) to repay the
bank overdraft; and (d) to contribute more than So. Sh. 5 million (US$800,000)
to the Central Budget over the period 1973-1980. It was assumed that the
Government's contribution to LDA would cease and that the Agency would con-
tinue to finance the Serum and Vaccine Institute and the Hides and Skins
Center from its own resources; this annual allocation would be about So.
Sh. 1.6 million (US$260,000). Furthermore, LDA would annually contribute
So. Sh. 100,000 to its staff incentive fund and about So. Sh. 150,000 to
its housing fund.
16.       With the implementation of the Project assumed to commence in 1974,
LDA's operations would increase considerably at full development in year six.
Sales would be about So. Sh. 49 million annually and cost of sales about
So. Sh. 38 million leaving a gross profit of about So. Sh. 11 million. Live-
stock inspection fees and other miscellaneous income would bring in approx-
imately So. Sh. 6 million giving a total gross income from all operations of
about So. Sh. 17 million. Net income after interest and depreciation would
amount to about So. Sh. 7 million with an adequate debt service ratio of
1.7x. (Annex 20, Table 1).
Summary
17.       LDA has now been in operation for seven years.  Although it has
developed some expertise in livestock trading it remains an inefficient
organization. In the past it mainly carried out designated Government
functions without adequate recompense, and the profitability of livestock
trading was determined to a great extent by prices received at the Kismayu
meat factory, another Government agency (Annex 4). The most important
constraints on more efficient operation are poor staffing, lack of expertise
in business organization and management, limited financial autonomy and
the absence of livestock production expertise. LDA's organization and
management would, however, be substantially improved under the proposed
Project (Annex 19).

SOMALIA
TRANS-JUBA LIVESTOCK PROJECT
The Kismayu Meat Factory
1.        The meat factory at Kismayu became operational in 1969.  It was
financed by the USSR at a total investment cost of So.Sh. 43 million
(US$6.9 million), with a provision that the Plant would be managed by a
team of Russian experts until 1976. Under the financing agreement the
Plant is required to export a total of 16 million round cans (250 grams each)
of stewed steak per year to the Soviet Union at a price of So.Sh. 1.68
(US$0.27) per can fob; negotiations are underway to increase this price
to So.Sh. 1.80 (US$0.29).
2.        The Plant has a total slaughtering capacity of 50 head/hour and
can process a maximum of about 250 head/day for canning. The Plant is
well designed and allows a good production flow, although in some respects
it does not reflect modern sanitary practices and standards. Construction
is of good quality with smooth concrete floors, adequate drains, tiled
walls and flat concrete slab ceilings. The equipment, in general, is well
maintained and most surfaces in contact with edible product are of stainless
steel.
3.        In 1972 the Plant not only met its quota for export to the Soviet
Union but also sold 600,000 cans of cooked corned beef (CCB) to African
and Mediterranean markets. The value of total sales was approximately
So.Sh. 30 million (US$4.8 million). The total kill in 1972 was 52,621 cat-
tle, with an average liveweight of 242 kg/head; dress-out yield was 44% and
boning yield for canning was 72%. Volume fluctuated from a minimum of 3,428
head in November to a maximum of 5,391 head in May (equivalent to 220 head/
day during this peak month).
Volume Under the Proposed Project
4.        At full development the proposed Project would significantly in-
crease the annual volume to be processed at the Plant. The increase would
include:
(a)  25,000 head of fattened cattle from the proposed
feedlot; these would average 370 kg/head and would
be intended for export, bone-in or boneless, in
chilled or frozen condition;
(b)  18,300 head of canner cattle similar to the cattle
currently processed at the plant, with an average
liveweight of 257 kg/head;



ANNEX 4
Page 2
(c) 600 head of canner cattle of lighter quality with
an average liveweight of 230 kg/head.
5.        About 25,000 head of the current canner volume of the Plant are
drawn from the Project area. With the improved stockroutes and watering
points, cattle transit losses would be reduced and average liveweight at
destination should increase from the present 242 kg to an estimated 257 kg.
The aggregate improvement in live weight of canner cattle now coming from
the Project area would thus amount to 375 tons.
Capacity Constraints
6.        The additional volume produced under the Project at full develop-
ment (in year six) would represent an increase of 84% over the number of
cattle slaughtered in 1972, and an increase of 127% in the production of
bone-in meat (assuming a dress-out yield of 50% for the feedlot cattle).
The availability of additional canner cattle would be subject to seasonal
fluctuations; in the peak month (May) the total daily kill would increase
from the present average of 220 head/day to 295 head/day. Feedlot cattle
would, however, be supplied evenly throughout the year, with an average
of 2,080 head per month or 80 head/day. With the additional volume under
the Project, the average daily kill during the peak months would thus be
375 head per day. In view of this increase there would be capacity
constraints in various operating areas of the plant, and expansion would
be required (Table 1).
7.        Cattle pens.  The present capacity of up to 1,000 head would be
sufficient to handle the increased volume as long as cattle are not held
over 48 hours. The individual liveweight scale would, however, be too
slow and a second scale would have to be provided.
8.        Killing floor.  The peak daily kill of 375 head per day could be
handled on the existing floor. However, larger work platforms, an addi-
tional carcass splitting saw, and head and viscera handling facilities would
have to be furnished; changes should also be made to provide a more efficient
allocation of existing space.
9.        Chill rooms.  There are 162 m of chill room railage available
in three coolers. Allowing time for loading and unloading, the actual
chilling cycle requires about 20 hours per day. The three existing coolers
would be allocated for canner beef 1/. One of the three coolers would,
however, have to be set aside for loading the fresh kill in the morning. The
remaining two coolers would have a capacity of 250 canners. This would be
sufficient to meet present needs (220 head), but would not be adequate to
allow for the increased volume of 295 head/day which would be generated under
1/ Hanging carcasses at the rate of 2-1/2 head per lineal meter would
allow a total capacity of 400 head per day.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
the Project. Another cooler would have to be constructed to provide for
this increase. An additional 80 head/day of heavier feedlot cattle would
also be made available under the Project. This would be exported as chilled
or frozen beef, and would have to be chilled in a separate cooler from the
canner beef. A second additional cooler would therefore have to be built.
Half of the 80 head/day feedlot cattle would be exported as chilled beef
and half as frozen, bone-in or boneless (Table 4).
10.       Freezing.  After chilling, a 48-hour freezing cycle would be
required. There are now two blast freezers, which have sufficient capacity
to freeze the 80 head/day of heavier feedlot cattle. Existing freezer
storage facilities have a capacity of 300 tons at -18C. This corresponds
to approximately 21 days production of bone-in carcass beef and 30 days of
boneless beef. Although frozen meat would possibly be air-freighted to
some markets, the freezer store capacity is too limited to provide for
increased requirements. The situation could be improved slightly by
converting an unused chamber which would be able to carry an additional
75 tons at -18C. Plans for increasing the freezer storage capacity to
1,000 tons by 1974 are now being developed by Government.
11.       Chiller beef.  As it is a highly perishable product, certain
precautions must be taken if feedlot beef is to be exported in chilled
form. Carcasses must be spaced at 1-1/2 head per m and kept in a separate
cooler from the canner beef. Spear temperatures must be brought down to
0C, which can only be reached by exposing the carcasses to a two-day
chilling cycle; this would curtail present capacity by the equivalent of
one cooler every other day.  To avoid building a third new cooler, an
existing holding room of equal capacity could be converted into a regular
chill room by installing the necessary refrigerating equipment. Chilled
beef (either 4s bone-in carcass or boneless cuts in cartons) would have to
be regularly air-freighted with a short storage period at the Plant.
Storage requirements (at 0C) could be allocated to a separate section of
the proposed expansion of the present refrigerated storage (para 10). If
the entire feedlot livestock outturn were to be marketed as chilled beef,
one of the freezers could be used as a reserve cooler for chilling or
holding by installing the necessary temperature controls.
12.       Meat Boning.  Existing facilities would be inadequate to handle
the 127% increase in the total meat boning volume which would result if
feedlot carcasses were marketed as boneless cuts. The room should be
expanded and refrigerated so as to maintain a maximum temperature of 10C
at all times. Additional stainless steel boning tables and related equip-
ment should also be provided.
13.       Stewed steak filling line.  Under present conditions the existing
line can process up to 240 head of canners per day in two shifts; this
estimate takes into account increased canner weights resulting from
improved stockroutes. This capacity would be sufficient to meet overall
requirements for the foreseeable future. As there is no ready market for



ANNEX 4
Page 4
stewed steak outside the USSR, it is projected that the line would process
only about 200 head/day - an amount sufficient to produce the 16 million
cans which the Plant is committed to ship to the USSR annually.
14.       New CCB filling line.  Once equipment on order from Germany is
installed, the line would have a capacity of 130 canners per day in two
shifts. CCB represents a production outlet for the canner cattle not
required for stewed steak; as a total of 295 head/day of canners are.
available, the present capacity should prove sufficient. However, two
sterilizing retorts would have to be added to handle the peak volume.
15.       By-products.  In order to process all the material from the in-
creased volume of heavier animals, an additional melter with percolating
pan, a grinder and a continuous press would be required.
Production Parameters
16.       On the basis of the expected volume to be generated as a result
of the Project, the following production program parameters appear feasible
during the peak month:
(a) 200 head/day to produce the 16 million cans of stewed
steak (250 grams each) for the USSR annually;
(b) additional canner cattle up to 95 head/day destined
mainly for CCB production;
(c) 80 head/day feedlot cattle to be exported fresh
bone-in or boneless, chilled or frozen.
Utilities
17.       There are 3 diesel-driven generators of 500 KVA capacity each;
these would provide adequate power generation and capacity to meet projected
needs. There are 3 boilers for steam generation with a capacity of
2,500 kg steam per hour; these are also completely sufficient for the
forseeable future. There are 4 ammonia compressors of 100,000 kcal each
and 1 of 115,000 kcal available to meet present refrigerator needs. Two
additional units of 100,000 kcal each would, however, be necessary to
maintain present stand-by capacity. This would be in addition to the
equipment provided above for expanded freezer facilities (para 10). Water
is supplied through a 6" line from the new municipal treatment plant.
Capacity is 364 m per hr or 96,000 U.s. gal/h, well in excess of the
consumption of the town of Kismayu and the plant combined.
Sanitation
18.       Sanitary conditions, whether with regard to general cleanliness,
work procedures or product handling, are unsatisfactory. Neither the
foreign experts nor the local supervisors have an adequate knowledge of
modern sanitary standards and procedures. Veterinary inspection is also



ANNEX 4
Page 5
inadequate, not only due to the performance of the officers themselves
but also as a result of limitations imposed by the antiquated design or
lack of necessary facilities, particularly on the killing floor. Wash-hand
basins and sterilizers are lacking. It is strongly recommended that
Government take immediate steps to upgrade veterinary standards and
sanitary practices.
Operating Procedures
19.       Neither the foreign experts nor local supervisors are fully
acquainted with proper meat processing plant procedures relating to
slaughtering, edible by-product handling, manipulation of product in the
coolers and freezers, and the processing of by-products. Furthermore,
the staff involved lack the knowledge of yields and procedures that would
permit upgrading of by-products to obtain a better return and reduce waste
to an acceptable minimum. At present many by-products are downgraded
either because they are not saved or because they are allowed to deteriorate
due to poor processing.
20.       The canning facilities were not in operation during the appraisal
mission's visit. It appears, however, that the most efficient canning
procedures are not always followed. The Plant has acquired Russian equip-
ment for producing CCB, including a French (HEMA) filler and a German
can-closing machine. The filler is sui:able for the round cans now used
for stewed steak. However, change parts for the truncated pyramidal cans
(12 oz and 7 oz) to be used for CCB production have been ordered; these
cans would be bought in France. The expanded CCB production would almost
certainly prove unprofitable unless steps are taken to ensure adequate
recuperation of the meat extract. Indications were given that the Italian
firm "Star" would supply extract making equipment, payment for which would
be made in CCB shipments.
21.       Except in 1972, the Plant has generally operated at a loss.  The
operating margin in that year was reportedly over So.Sh. 2 million (US$320,000),
which should be adequate to service the debt to the USSR on a long-term
basis. Financial projections have been made on the assumption that a similar
profit margin would be maintained. This would imply that the USSR commitment
would be met at an average fob price of So.Sh. 1.80 per can of stewed steak;
that price would be compatible with the price of So.Sh. 1.20/kg for canner
cattle delivered to the Plant assumed under the Project.
22.       Slaughtering and production projections for the Plant are indi-
cated in Tables 2, 3 and 4. At full development in Year Six it is assumed
that all feedlot cattle would be marketed as fresh beef - 50% as chilled
boneless cuts packed in cartons and 50% in frozen bone-in quarters wrapped
in polybags. The meat would be exported to African, Southern European and
Middle Eastern countries. As there is no market for stewed steak outside
the USSR, it is assumed that all canner cattle generated by the Project
would be processed into CCB; this would then be marketed by the Italian
firm "Star" through its own marketing organization.



ANNEX 4
'1 6
Financial Implications
23.       At full development total yearly sales revenues are estimated
to increase from a pre-Project level of So.Sh. 32.4 million (US$5.2 million)
to So.Sh. 70.1 million (US$11.3 million); the corresponding operating
margins are So.Sh. 2.3 million (US$370,000) and So.Sh. 6.8 million (US$1.1
million) respectively (Table 5). The entire production of the Plant would
be exported, except 50% of the by-products which would be sold locally.
Accordingly, total yearly export earnings would increase from a pre-Project
total of So.Sh. 31.3 million to So.Sh. 68.1 million. The incremental
exports of So.Sh. 36.8 million would be in convertible currency equivalent
to US$5.9 million 1/. Foreign exchange required for imported fuel and
production materials would account for an estimated US$2 million, leaving
the yearly projected net incremental export earnings at US$3.9 million.
The estimated cost of the expansion of the factory would be about
So.Sh. 5 million (US$0.8 million), of which the foreign exchange component
would be slightly over 80%.
1/ At present the major part of the meat factory's production is sold to
the Soviet Union under the financing agreement (para. 1).

SOMALIA
TRANS-JUBA LIVESTOCK PROJECT
Banking and Credit
1.        At independence in 1960, four foreign-owned commercial banks had
branches in Somalia: National and Grindlays, Banca di Roma, Banca di Napoli
and the Bank of Port Said. The Somali National Bank (SNB), organized in
1960, was given general control over the country's banking activities and
was also authorized to engage in commercial banking functions. The Credito
Somalo, another Government commercial banking institution, also performed
a dual function; it extended short-term credit and, through its Development
Loan Section, medium- and long-term credit. In 1968 a third Government
banking institution, the Somali Development Bank (SDB), was established to
provide financing to the private sector in the form of medium- and long-term
loan and equity participation. In 1968 Government decided to reorganize
and streamline the banking system.
2.        In May 1970, the Government nationalized the four private
commercial banks and from May to December 1970, these acted as agencies of
the Somali National Bank. On January 1, 1971, the combined assets and
liabilities of National and Grindlays, Banca di Roma, and Banca di Napoli
were merged to form the Somali Commercial (SCB). At the same time, the
Somali Saving and Credit Bank (SSCB) was formed from the consolidated assets
and liabilities of both the Bank of Port Said and the commercial banking
section of Somali National Bank.
3.        The two commercial banks are wholly state-owned, each having
a capital of So. Sh. 2.5 million equally subscribed by SNB and Government.
Both banks are engaged primarily in commercial banking operations and 24
branches of the new banks have been opened and are now in operation.
4.        Both commercial banks offer between 2-1/2 and 4-1/2% interest
annually on deposits depending on the period. Against this, the rate of
interest charged on short-term loans to the private sector is 9%. Govern-
ment agencies however enjoy preferential rates: the Livestock Development
Agency obtains credit at 6% to encourage the development of the livestock
sector; the Agricultural Development Corporation 5% and the Banana Board
(from SCB) at 7-1/2% annually to promote the development of the banana
industry.  The distribution of short-term credit granted by the Somali
banking system by types of economic activity as at December 31, 1971 shows
that trade (which also includes livestock trading) accounts for 71% of
total credit outstanding, industry and crafts 12% and agriculture 5%
(Table 1).
5.        The required security for loans is invariably either buildings or
personal guarantees; land alone is not acceptable since all land belongs to



ANNEX 5
Page 2
the state. This requirement limits the ability of the majority of farmers
and livestock traders to take advantage of the credit facilities offered
by the banks. However, livestock exporters may obtain credit to the extent
of 70% of the value of any opened letter of credit.
Somali Development Bank (SDB)
6.        The SDB was set up in March 1968 to provide financing to the
private sector in the form of medium- and long-term loans and equity
participation. In November 1968, following the liquidation of Credito Somalo,
the SDB took over that institution's Development Loan Section and is now
the only institution providing medium- and long-term loans. Subsequent to
the nationalization of the Somali banking system and other enterprises in
May 1970, the role of SDB was modified and it is presently committed to
support and finance sound and viable development projects originating in
the private as well as in the public sectors.
7.        SDB is controlled by a Board of Directors under the chairmanship
of a President who is appointed by Presidential decree for a term of five
years. The day-to-day operations of the Bank are the responsibility of the
General Manager, who is also appointed by Presidential decree on the
recommendation of the Board. SDB has two main departments: a technical
department and an administration department with a total staff of 25.
8.        SDB is prohibited from accepting savings or demand deposits, and
its only source of funds until recently has been its own equity paid up
capital of So. Sh. 20.6 million (US$3.3 million). 54% of this is held by
the Somali Government, 45% by the Somali National Bank and 1% by the Somali
Commercial Bank. In 1972 the Bank obtained a loan from Iraq of i 500,000
(US$1.3 million), repayable over 8 years at an annual interest of 2.52.
Another source of funds is the Somali Government which appropriated
So. Sh. 22 million (US$3.5 million) in its 1973 budget for this purpose.
Under Law No. 58 of July 1972, Government agencies are directed to create
depreciation funds to be paid over to the Ministry of Finance: these are
expected to be channelled through SDB and utilized for development purposes.
9.        In 1971 total SDB financing expanded to So. Sh. 7.0 million
(US$1.1 million), the same level as in 1969, after falling sharply in 1970
to So. Sh. 1.3 million (US$0.2 million). This improvement was attributed
to an improved business climate in the private sector after a period of
uncertainty following the change in Government and to the increased
operations of several public agencies whose role in industrial planning and
project implementation had not come up to expectations in the previous year.
Since under present circumstances private capital is cautious and entre-
preneurship is lacking, the Bank has been assigned the task of planning,
promoting and implementing viable projects to be eventually handed over
to prospective owners on a turn-key basis. Two such projects were promoted
in the first half of 1972. Although its activities are increasing, SDB's
operational efficiency is hampered by a shortage of qualified and experienced
staff and a scarcity of suitable projects.
