SOMALIA
TRANS-JUBA LIVESTOCK PROJECT
THE PROJECT AREA
1.        The proposed Project Area (Area) covers some 65,000 km2 and is
bounded in the east by the Juba River in the north and west by the Kenya
border and in the south east by the Indian Ocean. The Area includes some
80% of the Lower Juba and some 40% of the Upper Juba administrative regions.
On a geographical basis it can be divided roughly into two regions:
(a)  The Uplands Region                         Area in km2
Mander-El Wak Uplands                        20,000
Upper Juba Valley                             2,000
(b) The Central and Coastal Region
Dugiuma Sub-Region                            3,000
Marine Plain                                 12,000
Lower Juba-Shebeli Flood Plain                2,000
Fafadun Plain                                13,000
Lac Dera Plain                               11,000
Dudumali Plain                                2,000
Total Project Area                      65,000
2.        The Area can be broadly characterized as a relatively featureless
plain, interrupted by river valleys and tracts of rougher relief. For the
most part it is arid to semi-arid rangeland subject to recurrent droughts;
it has low shrubs and infrequent trees. Rainfall and soil variations are
reflected in the composition of the types and species of the shrubs and
trees. Rainfall in the two rainy seasons is unreliable and yearly or
seasonal averages can be misleading. Temperatures are high but relative
humidity is low. Water supplies for the most part are sparse, seasonal,
unreliable widely dispersed and often brackish and the only permanent
supply is the Juba River. Nevertheless, the Area contains an estimated
0.9 to 1.0 million head of cattle for which the main market outlet is the
Kismayu meat factory currently geared mostly to canning for exports.
Soils and Topography
3.        The tUplands Regions in the north is mostly hilly, with high country border-
ing Kenya to the northwest.  In general they are arid with sparse vegetation with a
limited grazing potential confined, because of sparse water supplies, to during and
shortly after the rains. Up to 25% of the total area is bare earth. It is estimated



ANNEX 6
Page 2
that the total annual forage production per km2 is about 14 tons, with an
annual carrying capacity of 4 animal units (cattle) per km2. Total grazing
area is about 5,600 km2 1/. Altitudes range from 300-500 meters above sea
level. Erosion has created a rolling to undulating pattern with scattered
erosional remnants. To the east lies the Marine Plain, a flat featureless
area with underlying marine sediment. The plain slopes gently towards the
south except where incised by the course of the Lac Dera drainage system
and around the Descek Uama 2/. The Area supports dense semi-arid woodland
which gives way over large tracts to an open shrub and tree steppe. The
hills are formed from calcarious stratifications and soils are shallow and
dry.
The Central and Coastal Region
4.        The Dugiuma Sub-Region, is a part of the Eluviated plain which
stretches as a band across the south central section of the Area from
west of the Juba River almost to the Shebeli River. Soils are mainly
clays but there are large areas of outwash, composed of sandy clay loams
overlying the clay. The vegetational pattern is grassland of a more open
type.
5.        The Marine Plain, of about 12,000 km2 has adequate rainfall for
good grass production and the grasslands cover some 600 km2. However,
dense woodlands and shrub steppe cover some 80% of the area reducing the
potential for forage production. It is estimated that the plain has an
annual carrying capacity of some 13 animal units (cattle) per km2 with a
fodder production of around 50 m tons per km2 3/.
1/   No general ecological study of Somalia is available though studies
have been made of limited areas, one of the former British Protec-
torate, and two of the inter-riverine area between the Juba and
Shebeli rivers. The agricultural and water survey carried out by
FAO in 1968 also contains much useful information on ecology and
using these studies together with observations of previous workers,
a broad classification of the Area is possible.
2/   "Desceks" - slack water depressions lying back from the river at
flood time. These depressions fill with water which recedes slowly
back towards the river.
3/   In areas of pure grass stand, Bothriochloa insculpta, Cenchrus
ciliaris, Eragrostis caespitosa, Hyparrhenia dissoluta and Sporobolus
helvolus are the common species though, Chloris, Brachiaria, Sorghum
and Echinochloa species are also to be found. In areas of dense tree
and bush cover - up to 70% coverage - the common grass species are
confined to Dactyloctenium aegyptium, Eragrostis caespitosa and
Sporobolus helvolus. Acacia, Cordia, Dobera and Grewia species
constitute the main tree and bush cover, with Sensevieria species
replacing much of the grass.



ANNEX 6
Page 3
6.        The land slopes gently towards the south giving way to the Fafadun
Plain which lies at an altitude ranging from 7 to 300 meters, the soils for
the most part being brown to dark-brown Solonetz, cracking when dry and
supporting a cover of semi-arid tree and shrub steppe. The Fafadun Plain,
covering some 13,000 km2, is generally speaking good open rangeland capable
of supporting some 11 animal units (cattle) per km2. Trees and shrubs,
mainly acacia species, comprise up to 30% of the total area. The area of
browse is estimated at 8,000 km2 and the annual estimated forage production
40 tons per km2 1/.
7.        West of the Marine Plain is the Lac Dera Plain which ranges in
altitude from 30 to 80 meters. The soils are considerably intermixed and
classified generally as Solonchak-Solonetz soils. The profiles exhibit
salinity and have a high sodium content throughout the sub-surface and sub-
soil horizons. The natural vegetation is used extensively for grazing or
browsing and supports a large domestic and wild animal population. The Lac
Dera Plain is characterized by its "park-like" appearance with grassland
and scattered tree and patches of tree shrub steppe. It is an area of good
natural rangeland. In the more open areas grass cover comprises over 90%
of the area. The plain is estimated to be capable of supporting 21 animal
units per km2 and producing 75 tons of forage per km2. The Dugiuma Sub-
Region provides good open grazing for the greater part but the bush tends
to become more demse in the west, the general ecology being the same as that
of the Marine Plain 2/.
8.        The Dudumali Plain, lying to the southern end of the Area, is
gently sloping and lies at an elevation of approximately 30 meters. The
soils are non-saline, fine textured, dark red to dusky red sandy clays
developcd in a shallow mantle of weathered coral limestone underlain by
weathered coral at depths which are variable but most commonly exceed 50 cm.
The naturaA. vegetation is semi-arid tree and shrub steppe. Lying immediately
behind the shore line is the sand dune area comprising stabilized and active
sand dunes. The areas are of little significance but provide grazing for
sheep and goats on the stabilized areas a topographical feature common to
all areas is the Juba River which forms the eastern boundary. Along the
riverine belt are deeper alluvial soils which support a heavy tree and
shrub cover.
1/   The common grass species are Bothriochloa insculpta, Cenchrus
ciliaris, Eragrostis caespitosa, Hyparrhenia dissoluta and
Sporobolus helvolus. Chloris, Brachiaria and Echinochloa species
are also to be found. The main tree and shrub species are:
Acacia bussei, Acacia etbaica, Acacia nilotica and Ehretia.
2/   The tree and shrub species comprise Acacia millifora, Acacia
sperocarpa, Commiphera, Cordia, Grewia and Terminalia species.



ANNEX 6
Page 4
Communications
9.        Roads in the area are generally poor and the majority are impass-
able to vehicles for up to five months or more every year. With the con-
centration of irrigated banana farming in the area between Gelib and Kismayu,
however, a 114 km paved road connecting these two towns has recently been
completed. All the administrative centers are linked by roads of varying
quality, but rivers present a barrier; with the exception of the bridge
across the Juba River at Gelib, the only other crossing points are ferries
at Lugh Ferrandi and Bardera.
10.       Kismayu harbor has recently been reconstructed by USAID and is
now capable of simultaneously handling two banana boats of 2,000-4,000 gross
ton and two other vessels up to 10,000 gross ton along side the deep water
docks. The reconstruction involved the building of cargo sheds, adminis-
trative buildings, cold storage and freezing facilities; the provision of
equipment such as a tug, forklift and trucks; and the installation of a
good water supply. Kismayu, the regional administrative center, is served
by the DC-3 service of Somali Airlines and the recently improved landing
strip is now all-weather. A new airport capable of handling the biggest
passenger and cargo planes is under construction and will be completed in
1975. The only other landing strip for light aircraft is at Afmadu. All
administrative center and police stations are interconnected by wireless,
but police facilities are confined to Kismayu. The inhabitants of these
towns are mostly employed as shop and restaurant-keepers, traders, etc. and
maintain a few cattle and a small plot of land for subsistence purpose.
11.       The settlers who farm along the banks of the Juba River, although
generally static, still maintain small herds of livestock. These people
provide one of the main sources of agricultural labor on the banana planta-
tions - the only large employers of labor within the Project Area.  It is
estimated that these estates give employment to some 8,000 people.
12.       In the past the nomadic livestock were designed not pre-
dominantly for sale but to satisfy needs for prestige, meat, animal fat,
milk and the transport of family goods. Attitudes are now changing, how-
ever. The desire for better educational and medical facilities is now
strong and there is a greater tendency to establish permanent homes near
such facilities, keeping a small herd for subsistence while grazing the
remainder under the traditional pattern. Semi-permanence instils greater
material needs, and the herdsman now tends to look upon his livestock as
potential income and will sell animals readily at the most advantageous time
rather than in emergency brought about by droughts or other natural catas-
trophies.
October 10, 1973.



ANNEX 7
Page 1
SOMALIA
TRANS-JUBA LIVESTOCK PROJECT
CATTLE MARKETS, STOCKROUTES AND HOLDING GROUND
1.        The proposed Project would establish organized marketing outlets
for livestock in the Project Area in order to increase off-take rate and
reduce losses. The additional infrastructure would facilitate the orderly
purchase, movement and storing of cattle, and allow extension of the neces-
sary veterinary supervision to a much larger number of cattle.
Cattle Markets
2.        Three new permanent cattle markets (Tabda, Maskati and Gelib) would
be located so as to service those areas with the greatest concentration of
cattle. The markets are simple in design (Plan), and one each would be
established in Project years one, two and three. The site in Gelib would
replace the one originally proposed in Garsei Warshan; the former was
selected because of its proximity to the existing EEC holding ground east
of the Juba River and the very poor road conditions in the Garsei Warshan
area. The choice of sites is tentative, however, and the final decision
would be left to Project Management. LDA (from its existing financial
resources) has recently constructed one additional market at Badada.
3.        It is estimated that the majority of about 30,000 head of
Somali cattle presently marketed each year in Kenya enter it in the
south-western corner of the Project Area (Annex 2, para 3). The pro-
posed markets would gradually reduce this flow. An undetermined num-
ber of cattle, however, move across the border towards the north of
the Project Area. It is proposed that two smaller markets be estab-
lished at Lugh Ganana and Busar in an effort to provide incentives for
nomads to sell more cattle locally, and also as a means of exploring
the feasibility of establishing more permanent markets. These would
operate either periodically or until it was known whether the estab-
lishment of more permanent and regular markets would be justified.
4.        These markets, to be constructed in Project years two and three,
are much the same design as the larger markets, although on a reduced scale.
They are capable of future expansion should this prove feasible. Market
construction would include housing for the market teams. The markets would
be constructed and maintained by the Project Construction and Maintenance
Unit (Tables 1 and 4).
Stockroutes
5.        About 1,000 km of mostly existing stockroutes would be improved
to connect the permanent markets with the holding grounds at Afmadu and



ANNEX 7
Page 2
Kismayu. This would be done by establishing simple staging posts every 30 km
- each comprising three thornbrush paddocks and drovers' shelters. A water
point would be stablished at each staging post, either by re-activating
existing boreholes, establishing river pumping units or constructing new
boreholes, stock ponds (of 20,000 m3 and known locally as "Uars") or dams
(Tables 2 and 4).
6.        Some 450 km of the proposed stockroutes have been surveyed in
detail and the balance is under survey and would be completed before dev-
elopment commences. The Project would provide a phase development program
over four years, and the Water Development Agency or local private contract-
ors would construct new or renovate existing boreholes. The Project Con-
struction and Maintenance Unit would carry out the remaining work.
7.        In order to facilitate the movement of cattle from the EEC holding
ground and the new Gelib market to Kismayu, the existing ferry approaches
would be renovated. Replacement of the existing ferry is not warranted as
carrying capacity and operating condition are satisfactory. Also included
in the cost of developing the stockroutes are minor improvements to parts
of the Badada-Kismayu road and to the road linking the Kismayu holding ground
with the town. These improvements are limited to installing culverts and
drifts, and making realignments so as to afford the marketing staff better
access during the rainy seasons.
Holding Ground
8.        Because the existing LDA operated cattle holding ground at Kismayu
is already overloaded, the Project would establish a new 20,000 ha holding
ground between Afmadu and Dif, about 10 km north of Beles Gogani, where
there is a good probability of obtaining reliable supplies of potable water
from boreholes. The holding ground would comprise a perimeter fence sub-
divided by firebreaks into nine paddocks - roads, three boreholes, one
cattle dip, three sets of yards, housing for ground attendants and a market
team, office, stores, thorn-brush night pens for the cattle, and operating
equipment (Tables 3 and 4).
9.        The carrying capacity of this holding ground is estimated at
eight ha per animal or around 2,500 head per year. Since cattle would
normally only be held for about one month, the throughput would be around
30,000 head per year. The holding ground would be used primarily for
regulating the supply of slaughter cattle to Kismayu but would also be used
for the selection and sorting of immatures for the two grazing ranches and
the Irrigated Fodder Farm and Feedlot. On arrival at Kismayu, slaughter
stock will go through the normal quarantine and veterinary procedures and
will be held until dispatched for export or for slaughter at the meat plant.
To facilitate export of live cattle, a weighbridge is to be installed at
the Kismayu marshalling yard.



ANNEX 7
Page 3
Market Teams
10.       To improve the marketing system, it is necessary to strengthen
the field marketing section of LDA's Kismayu Field Unit. For this purpose
the Project includes three new cattle purchasing teams: A mobile team
would be located at the new Afmadu holding ground and operate the markets
at Tabda and Maskati; the other teams would be based at Lugh and Busar and
would separately operate these markets.  Each team would consists of a
market master, brander, head herdsman and vaccinator. The mobile team would
also have a clerk, sorter and driver. An existing marketing team located
at the Kismayu holding ground would operate the markets at Hadweineh and
Gelib and also purchase cattle at the Afmadu town market. Supervision of
the new marketing teams would be the responsibility of an Assistant Marketing
Officer stationed on the Afmadu Holding Ground, assisted by an animal health
assistant, senior clerk and driver.
11.       The projected number of cattle to be purchased at each market,
based on estimated concentration of livestock, and the manpower required for
herding is shown in Table 5. The calculation of herdsmen requirements has
been based on regular market purchases, driving the cattle to holding grounds
and their subsequent movement from the holding grounds to the port or to
the meat factory in Kismayu.
12.       A Ranch Manager would be in charge of the holding ground in
Afmadu, assisted by the necessary maintenance and operating staff and herds-
men. Purchased stock would be moved down stockroutes to holding grounds,
depending on actual numbers being held or grazing capacity, and cattle would
be moved as and when required to the Kismayu holding ground or the ranches.
August 17, 1973

Page 1
SOMALIA
TRANS-JUBA LIVESTOCK PROJECT
Irrigated Fodder Farm and Feedlot - Irrigation Component
The Juba River Basin
1.        The Juba River is the largest perennial river in Somalia.  Its head-
waters lie in the highlands of Ethiopia in the north, where it receives most
of its flow from three main tributaries; the Uebi Gestro, the Canal2 Doria and
the Daua Parma. The river has a watershed area of about 275,000 km , 98,000 of
which are within Somalia. The river in Somalia follows a general north-south
direction, finally discharging into the Indian Ocean near Kismayu.
2.        Permanent gauging stations were established by FAO in 1963 at Lugh
Ganana (731 km from the river mouth), Bardera (496 km), Kaitoi (220 km), and
Gimama (59 km).  Synthetic hydrographs at these stations were computed for the
period 1951 to 1964. Flow records were not kept after 1965. 1/
3.        Monthly flow values at Gimama, about 30 kilometers downstream of the
irrigated fattening farm site, are shown in Table 1. The mean areual dis-
charge recorded at this station is 172 cubic veters per second (m /sec), with
maximum average monthly flows exceeding 400 m /sec and minimum flows of 2m /sec.
The proposed point of irrigation water diversion lies on one of two channels
of the Juba river bifurcated by Mombasa Island at a point about 4 kilometers
upstream. Flow proportion estimates through these channels have not as yet
been made. Examination of aerial photographs available in the area indicates
that the major channel lies west of Mombasa Island where extraction will
take place. It is estimated that 75% of the total flow will pass through
this channel.
4.        Samples of Juba River water were taken at the Gimama station by FAO
and others. The chemical quality of the water in general is good for irriga-
tion. Although total dissolved solids are higher during the low flow months
and in the initial flushing stage preceding high flows, a portion of these
salts are gypsum (Calcium sulfate), which Is the most common chemical addi-
tive used to reclaim alkali soils. Its use increases permeability, and there-
fore use of the Juba water for irrigation may improve soil conditions. Vari-
ous samples reveal that although the alkali hazard of the water is low, the
salinity hazard is high for sensitive crops such as tree fruits and bananas,
but low for most grain and forage crops, an important factor for this project.
1/ In October 1969 the stations at Lugh, Bardera and Gimama were rehabilitated.
Intermittent water level records are available for these stations for the
period October 1969 to Devember 1972 but are not believed to be of high
standard due to the absence of reliable field transport.



ANNEX 9
Page 2
5.        A soils survey in the Lower Juba area was first conducted by the
former International Cooperation Administration (now USAID) and published in
their report "Inter-River Economic Exploration - the Somali Republic" in
1961. This survey was later extended by FAQ from 1962 to 1966, and by
Selchozpromexport of Moscow in 1965. Soils classification maps for the Lower
Juba basin are now available. The Lower Juba area is divided into six major
land classes (I to VI) ranked in terms of arability and suitability for ir-
rigation. The availability of Class I level (arable: very good for irrigation)
was limited, since most of this land has been developed for banana plantations.
The selection of a suitable site for the irrigated ranch had therefore to
be confined to Class II land (arable: good for irrigation). The surface
soils on the proposed irrigated fattening farm are clay loam with a silty
clay to clay texture. They are moderately well drained, and have an effective
depth of over 150 cm. The salinity of alkali content is negligable. Never-
theless, without drainage the subsoils would probably contribute to the salin-
ity and alkalinity of the surface soils. Therefore, along with a program
of green manure, deep plowing, and fertilization with nitrogen and phosphates,
a drainage system is a necessity.
6.        The air temperature in the Lower Juba region is high throughout the
year. The yearly 12 months average is about 28C, Lowest in July 25'C and
highest in March 30'C. This is the most important single factor that governs
the water consumptive use of plants. Estimated water consumptive use at the
proposed site is shown in Table 2. The high potential evapotranspiration rates
result in a net water deficit for crop consumptive use in every month of the
year. Irrigation is therefore essential for feed production to supply the
fattening operation.
Existing State of Irrigation Practice
7.        Despite its high annual flow, very little use is presently made of
the waters of the Juba River in Somalia. This is due to the fact that live-
stock has been the mainstay of the country's economy and agriculture has been
confined to a restricted area. The absence of tributary streams in the Lower
Juba valley requires all water for irrigation to be extracted from the river
itself, and this has limited irrigation to a narrow strip of land along the
river banks. The high cost of water conveyance to areas located away from
the river precluded small scale irrigated farming in these areas; where
land is fertile, rainfed crops - principally maize - are planted with rather
poor yi"elds.
8.        Irrigation is mainly confined to about 5,000 ha devoted to banana
plantations; the plantations line both banks of the river to a width of
about two to three kilometers, and occupy the most fertile areas in the
region. Individual plantations differ in size, and some larger plantations
extend up to 100 ha. The water is supplied by means of semi-portable
dieseldriven horizontal centrifugal pumps, usually of standard manufacture,
located at the river bank. The pumped water is discharged immediately into
a stilling basin leading into an elevated ditch for conveyance to the fields.
Usually no shelter is provided for the pumping equipment.



ANNEX 9
Page 3
9.        During the early years of irrigation when there was no form of drain-
age, the salts in the subsoils evidently entered the soil-water solution of the
surface soil causing a gradual reduction in crop production.  In most of the
plantations is has been necessary to install deep drainage systems at intervals
as close as 30 meters; in this way soils could be leached by excessive irriga-
tion to restore and retain higher production levels.
10.       The Agricultural Development Corporation, under the Ministry of
Agriculture, operates a small 55 ha fodder farm near the village of Zunguni
for the production of hay and hay pellets used in shipping live cattle for
export. The main crop sown is Sudan grass. The land was not properly
levelled prior to cultivation, and this has required irrigation water to be
applied on divided small plots over an area of about 25 m . There is no
established crop watering pattern, and production is poor.
11.       Proposals for the large-scale use of water from the Juba River were
first advanced in 1961 by USAID and subsequent studies were conducted by FAO
and Selchozpromexport of Moscow (para. 5). The proposals involved the eventual
development of irrigation systems to serve existing and new farm lands on an
area of some 150,000 ha. The first phase of this scheme has now been approved
by Government; it involves construction of an irrigation dam at Fanole and
development of 8,300 ha of state farms for the production of cotton and oil
seeds.
12.       A proposal has also been put forward by FAO for the development of
3,000 ha of irrigated farms in the Afgoi-Mordile area using water from the
Shebeli river. Crops would be cotton, groundnuts and upland rice and a
pilot project of 108 ha is now under construction.
13.       Irrigation farming is still in its early stages in Somalia.  Its
development however is advancing, as it is evident that the country's agri-
cultural potential could be better realized by making more efficient use of
the water now flowing unused into the ocean.
I Description of the Irrigation System
14.       Government had selected and put forward in its project proposal a
possible site for the irrigated fodder farm and feedlot. The proposed loca-
tion seems to be favorable as basic requirements such as topography, soil
quality distance from the river and the paved highroad are secured. However,
a survey of alternative locations could prove that better sites exist. The
engineering conslutants (Appendix 1 para 2) determine the final location of
the irrigated farm and to prepare the design of the irrigation system which
would follow the preliminary schemes outlined below. The proposed irrigation
system would occupy a gross area of some 1,300 ha out of which a net area of
1,200 ha would be irrigated. In addition a feedlot and building site of
about 15 ha would be located adjacent to the irrigated area and near the
Kismayu-Gelib highway. Water would be pumped from the Juba River into a sup-
ply canal and conducted under the highway by an inverted syphon. The water
would then be divided at the northeast corner of the farm and distributed to
the fields through a primary, two secondary, and two tertiary canals (Map 10430 R)



ANNEX 9
Page 4
The main supplv canal would run from the pumping site for an estimated
distance of 2 km.  (Map IBRD 104309).
15.       Four vertical pumps, each capable of delivering 440/sec. (7,000
U.S. gal/min), would be housed in a pumping station designed with a small
work shop and a storeroom for spare parts, lubricants and other maintenance
material. Each pump would be powered by a direct connected diesel engine
capable of delivering 104 hp continuously at 15 m above sea level. Considera-
tion was given to use of electric powered pumps from one generator. However
this scheme was discarded because of the higher investment and operating costs,
and the fact that even though a standby pump would exist, a breakdown of the
generator would de-commission all of the pumps. Water requirements were com-
puted using the Blaney-Creddle formula (Table 2). An overall irrigation ef-
ficiency of 65% was used which takes into consideration ditch losses, evapora-
tion and deep percolation. Although 70% efficiency is possible under furrow
irrigation, this figure would probably not be attained - at least during the
early years of operation. It can be seen from Table 2 that peak demand occurs
during the month of January and requires a pumping capability of 2.89 million
cubic meters; this means that three pumps operating three shifts per day and
26 days per month would be able to meet the demand. Under maximum conditions
at least one pump would be on standby in case of a breakdown. The pump house
should be of reinforced concrete frame with steel mesh between columns, and with
a trussed roof covered with corrugated metal or asbestos cement. Base walls and
partitions should be concrete block. A concrete intake with trash rack and head-
gate would lead from the river to the pump gallery. Each pump sump should have
a gate or stop logs so that it can be dewatered when repairs are underway. The
building should be designed so that the floor and pump bases, as well as the
discharge structure and canal, would well above anticipated flood level (high-
water marks in the vicinity of the site are 35 cm above ground level.) The
designated point of diversion is on the branch of the Juba River west of
Mombassa Island and some seven kilometers downstream from the highway bridge
near Cansuma.
16.       Soils in the area have a low seepage factor making it unnecessary
to line the canals. The supply canal and a portion of the primary canal
should be in embankment section for flood protection as well as functional
purposes. Other canals would be in balanced cut and fill section, designed
with the water level above the natural ground so that a minimum of checking
wouild be required. Canal structures such as division boxes, turnouts, etc.
would be of reinforced concrete. Due to the topography, drop structures are
not contemplated.
17.       The fields would generally be irrigated by the border method,
either ditch or dyke, depending upon the topography after levelling and the
experience of the irrigators. The pasture should be irrigated by the border
dyke method unless the side slope is excessive where border ditches' would
be used. Corn would be irrigated bv border ditches, furrows or corrugations,
depending on whether it is planted for silage (border ditches or corrugations)
or grain (furrows). Borders should be spaced as far apart as possible,
depending on the side slope, and irrigation runs should not exceed 400 meters
in length wfith well-graded surface drains at the end of each run; in this way



ANNEX 9        y
Page 5
as much surface water as possible is carried away before reaching the subsoils
and thus contributing to any subsurface drainage problem that may exist. Con-
sideration was given to sprinkler irrigation, but due to excessive cost and
the high winds which are a feature during extended periods during the year,
this idea was abandoned.
18.       Available soil analyses and profiles indicate a necessity for a
subsurface drainage system. Field drains would be open ditches not less
than 1.5 m deep and spaced at 100 m intervals. These ditches would lead
into primary drains leading into the main outlet; the water would then dis-
charge into an existing drainage canal and finally return to the Juba River.
Similar drainage systems are successful on adjacent banana plantations, al-
though some field drains are spaced at closer intervals. Since the land,
being newly irrigated, will not have any salt concentrations in the top
soils, and will have efficient surface drainage under good management, the
planned interval should be satisfactory. A subsurface open tile system
was considered, but was abandoned because of excessive investment cost.
19.       In addition to the access road to the pumping plant, there would
be 28 kIn of farm roads. The access road would lead from the Kismayu-Gelib
highway to the pumping site on the river bank and would be approximately
0.6 km in length. All roads would be surfaced the full width of 5 m with
gravel 15 cm deep. Road ditches would have 3:1 in-slopes and back-slopes
and would be used as head ditches and surface drains when the head and end
of irrigation runs coincide with road locations. The road system provides
access to the highway from the farm and feedlot at two points (Map 10430R).
20.       Water for cattle and domestic consumption would be supplied from
the irrigation systen to the feedlot at the northwest corner. The water
for domestic use would have to be clarified and treated. It has been as-
sumed that this would be done by the use of a small package plant consisting
of clarifier, pressure filter and hypochlorinator.
21.       The major technical components of the engineering works cf the
irrigation component are described below:

Ecology
22.       The irrigated fattening farm is small enough that it will have
no appreciable effect on the ecology of the area. However, Schistosomiasis
(Bilharzia) and Mlalaria are both prevalent in the area. Since the schis-
tomes (parasitic worms) are transmitted by fresh water snails and malaria
parasites are carried by the anapheline mosquito (which uses water for a
breeding place), any water development would tend to spread the disease
unless precautions were taken.
Schistosomiasis
23.       Since the parasites that cause this disease are carried by fresh
water snails, some control measures should be taken. Generally the host
snails are rarely seen in streams having velocities exceeding 30 to 35
cm/sec. However, even though the mean velocity exceeds the above figure,
peripheral velocities in canals, for instance, may be such that the snails
would cling to rough edges or marginal vegetation. The snails would probably
also abound in areas of still water. Therefore, it is recommended that:
(a) The consulting engineer design canals so that velocities
exceed thirty-five cm/sec, (for purposes of this report
preliminary design canal velocities range for 40 to 80
cm/sec but not high enough to cause erosion);
(b) The canals and drains be kept free of aquatic and other
plants that will shelter the snails;
(c) Irrigation be carried out so as to ensure efficiency and
prevent over-irrigation and consequent ponding.
24.       Consideration has been given to installing snail traps at the
entrances to all structures, but experience on other projects has shown
that to be of limited value, as well as the high cost involved in main-
tenance of the traps and disposal of the snails. The use of chemicals
(molluscides) is one of the most effective methods for snail control.
However, if the measures recommended above are carried out and are suc-
cessful, mollusciding may not be necessary.
Malaria
25.       Since the presence of still water is the common denominator for
both Malaria and Schistosomiasis, the same recommendations, if carried out,
would minimize the contributions of this Project site to any increase in the
prevalence of this disease. However, in the case of both diseases appro-
priate chemicals for control of the vectors should be used should environmental
controls envisaged prove inadequate.
26.       It should again be pointed out that the location and extent of the
irrigated fattening farm is such that any control measures will have little



ANNEX 9
Page 8
effect on the prevalence of these diseases in the general area, where at
present no control measures are taken or contemplated by the Government.
Project Schedule
27.       The program is shown in the attached work schedule (Chart).  The
irrigation component would require a total time of some two years, actual
construction would be underway of about one year excluding the bush clearing.
28.       Consultants would be required to advise LDA on the engineering
aspect of the irrigation component. This work would comprise topographical
surveys, detailed design and supervision of construction. The topographical
survey would include details of soil survey1/ and water sampling 2/. The
appointment of consulting engineers, to be selected according to IDA guide-
lines, would be a condition of Credit effectiveness.
29.       The design of the pumping equipment should be given priority so that
procurement of these items would be initiated at least seven months prior to
scheduled installation (Chart). In view of this necessity, the supply of
pumping equipment would not be required of the construction contractor but
would be included in a separate supplier's contract and procured under inter-
national competitive bidding. All design work and contract documents would
be completed in about four months; necessary.Somali and IDA approvals as well
as tendering procedures would tak about five months.  Actual construction
would probably begin about five months aftericompletion of tender documents.
30.       Construction of the pumping plant should proceed on strict schedule,
as the rainy season and consequent high water flows in the Juba river will
interfere with progress and could cause serious delays as well as higher
costs (Chart).
31.       Construction on the drainage system should be initiated at an
early stage, especially on the outlet and primary drains, so that at least
1/   A general soil survey of reconnaissance type is available for the Project
area. However, a more detailed soil survey with density of about one pit
every 25 ha is required as its result might influence the technical design
of the system, specifically the land levelling. According to available
soil classification the Project area is almost homogenous and belongs
to Class II that is classified as arable land good for irrigation.
2/   In the period 1963-1965 thirteen water samples were taken and analyzed
for the main chemical characteristics. However systematic sampling
during one dry, and one rainy season is required with subsequent more
detailed analysis as it could influence the sequence of irrigation.
One year observation would be sufficient as the incidence of irregularity
concerning dry and rainy seasons so far has been low.



ANNEX 9
Page 9
these elements would be able to function during the rainy season prior to
construction completion. Construction of the field drains should proceed
as the fields are completed, but primaries and outlets should be completed
before irrigation commences so that drainage will be functioning from the
beginning. This is necessary so that if any leaching becomes necessary
the drainage process can begin before concentrated salt solutions build
up in the top soils.
Staff and Operating Requirements
32.       The irrigation staff requirement would built up over four years.
A staff of four would be required, including provisions for relief required
from sickness and vacation.
33.       The basic irrigator team would consist of three, one of whom would
be gangleader. At full staffing, nine gangs would be required for an eight
hour day. An additional gang would be included for relief duties. The staff-
ing would be required to carry out minor repair and maintenance work during
periods when irrigation and pumping were not in process. The teams would be
directly supervised by three foremen. Labor requirements for this activity
would be included in the staffing of the Irrigated Fodder Farm and Feedlot.
34.       The pumping station would be manned by two pump operators and one
operator-mechanic. The operator-mechanic would be in charge of the pumping
plant and would oversee all the plant employees to ensure that the pumps
would be operated as scheduled by the irrigation specialist. All routine
repairs and maintenance would be performed at the pumping plant by pumping
plant employees, but extensive overhauling would be done by the Irrigated
Fodder Farm and Feedlot repair shop under the direction of the Master
Mechanic. A laborer would be assigned to the pumping plant to conduct
such regular maintenance work as desilting of the intake channel, clearing
of trash from the trash tacks, minor repair work, and other manual duties.
35.       Irrigation activities would be headed by an internationally recruited
irrigation specialist for the first three and one half years of operation. A
Somali would be trained overseas in irrigation management for a period of two
years and would return to take over after one year the management and all the
irrigation activities of the Irrigated Fodder Farm and Feedlot. Draft Terms
of Reference for the Irrigation Specialist are shown in Annex 19, Appendix 1.
36.       Sufficient fuel would be stored at the station to allow the pumps
to run at full capacity for at least one and one half weeks. Refueling
would be necessary at weekly intervals.
37.       Adequate spare parts for pumps and other equipment would be kept
in storage facilities provided in the pumphouse. The consultant responsible
for the design would be required to provide a comprehensive list of spare
parts required together with the necessary re-ordering levels.



ANNEX 9
Page 10
38.       Minor repairs to buildings and structures would be carried out by
the irrigation staff.  Operating cost estimates include funts for the pur-
chase of construction material and rental of equipment. Repairs for flood a
and storm damage or other unpredicted causes would be carried out by either
the farm staff or a local contractor, depending on the extent of the damage.
Cost and Expenditure
39.       The total capital cost for the full irrigation system (1,200 ha)
including engineering is estimated to be So.Sh. 10.4 million (US$1.7 million)
or So.Sh. 8,700 (US$1,400) per ha without contingencies. This estimate is
based on 1973 prices (Table 3).
40.       Operating costs would amount to So.Sh. 440,000 (US$70,000) for
two years of operation during the first three Project development years
(Table 4).  Fuel cost is based on a consumption of 0.1944 liters per
hp/hour at So.Sh. 1.10 per 1. At full operation the cost for supplying
water to the Irrigated Fodder Farm and Feedlot would be So.Sh. 417,000
(US$67,000) per year (Table 4).

Consultants Draft Terms of Reference for Engineering Aspects 1/
1.        An international firm of consultants would be selected and appointed
by the Livestock Develoument Agency (LDA) to implement the Irrigation com-
ponent of the Irrigated Fodder Farm and Feedlot. Their responsibilities
would essentially be as follows:
2.        The proposed area by Government for the irrigated fodder farm and
feedlot might not be the best solution regarding topography, water supply,
communications and construction costs. To this end the first task of the
firm would be to determine the best location of the farm.
Topographic Survey
3.        The topographic survey would consist of the necessary surveys for
the location of the Irrigated Fodder Farm and Feedlot (referred to in para-
graph 2 above) in particular to perform a more detailed soil survey within
the farm area, and take water samples from the Juba river (paragraph 28).
Specifically it would:
(a) Make a topographic survey of the area designated by
LDA to be the Irrigated Fodder Farm and Feedlot. The
survey would be based on a grid system 30 meters on
centers;
(b) Prepare a map showing principal features and contours
at 30 centimeter intervals;
(c) Perform a soil survey of the area for irrigable land
classification purposes and also to determine the
soil profile to a minimum depth of 150 centimeters;
(d) Prepare chemical analyses of the water samples;
(e) Prepare a report on the Juba River water reflecting
the information obtained from the chemical analyses;
1/ An international firm of consultants would preferably be employed to
carry out the work required for the engineering aspects of the irriga-
tion component and to fill the six posts required to execute and manage
the Project during development (Annex 19, Appendix 1).

(f) Make soil analyses to determine the chemical and
physical properties of the soils;
(g) Prepare a soils map reflecting the above survey;
(h) Take water samples from the Juba river at the pro-
posed pumping plant site at weekly intervals nmd
when significant changes in the river flow occur,
for a period of eight months including at least
one dry and the main rainy season.

(5) Road system with structures, (6) Domestic and

ANNEX 10
Page   1
SOMALIA
TRANS-JUBA LIVESTOCK PROJECT
Irrigated Fodder Farm and Feedlot - Production Component
1.        Somalia's economic future depends essentially on the development
of the agricultural sector; exports of livestock and livestock products to-
gether with bananas, now account for about 90% of the country's export
earnings. At present there are no cattle operations in Somalia and in the
absence of by-products the irregular rainfall (200-500 mm/annum) and re-
current droughts makes it necessary to produce suitable feeds through an
irrigation scheme. Establishment of an irrigated fodder farm and feedlot
would represent an important step in the modernization of the Somali cattle
industry. The irrigated fodder production proposed calls for an intensive
mechanized system since it is unlikely that sufficient labor of the required
quality could be found to operate the farm on a labor intensive basis.
However this opinion is necessarily preliminary and it may be that suffi-
cient labor could be obtained to replace a significant amount of the me-
chanization. Accordingly the consultants terms of reference would include
a requirement to investigate this possibility (Annex 19, Appendix 1). How-
ever, the funds necessary for a completely mechanized system are provided
under the Project.
2.        The feedlot operation would establish a demand for young immature
cattle passing through the grazing ranches; produce a regular supply of qual-
ity carcasses for export irrespective of the season, and help rural employ-
ment. Furthermore, if the feedlot operation were expanded in the future it
would provide a market for possible grain surpluses and other agricultural
by-products (molasses, oilseed cake, cotton seed hulls) and would not require
expansion of the irrigated area. The proposed feedlot would complement
investments in cattle marketing facilities and abattoirs. The efficient
utilization of these components is very much dependent on a volume of cattle
adequate to guarantee revenues which would cover fixed overhead costs.
Finally, the irrigated fodder farm, combined with the feedlot would have an
important catalytic role in changing the outlook of the traditional nomadic
producers.
Project Development
3.        On completion of the irrigation system in year two, the area 1,200
ha would be gradually brought into cultivation. The remaining 100 ha would
be devoted to roads, canals, buildings and the feedlot. Following land clear-
ing, initial levelling, and installation of irrigation canals, 200 ha would



ANNEX 10
Page 2
be sown to crop every six months, and the final 400 ha would be developed
simultaneously. More rapid phasing of development may prove possible, but
due to the nature of the soils, climatic conditions and the physical effort
and organization required, a conservative approach has been adopted for plan-
ning purposes.
4.        Total investment cost exclusive of initial land clearing and level-
ling and the irrigation systen would be about So.Sh. 11.4 million (US$1.8
million), or about So.Sh. 8,800 (US$1,300) per ha (Table 1). Investment
costs include machinery and plant (So. Sh. 6.7 million), buildings (So. Sh. 3.2
million) feedlot (So. Sh. 1.4 million), and cattle handling facilities and
water supply (So.Sh. 0.1 million. Because of the complexity of the
proposed operation and the necessity for timely completion of all pro-
duction operations, further substitution of capital intensive machinery
and labor would not be practicable. Full provision for machinery repair
facilities and spare parts supply has been made. Tables 1, 2, 3, 4
and 5).
Crop Rotation
5.        Soils, although suitable for irrigated crop production, are deficient
in organic matter. To ensure that the assumed crop yields would be obtained,
a quick growing leguminous crop, mung beans, would be ploughed in as green
manure before the first crop of corn was sown. This would take place in year
two to four, covering 400 ha annually. Corn would be followed by pasture
consisting of Alfalfa and Rhodes grass which would be maintained for 1 1/2 - 2
years before the area would again be cropped for corn.
6.        This initial cropping and pasture phase combined with both animal
manure and chemical fertilizer, would provide the fertility buildup required
to sustain the final rotation of 2 1/2 years - or 5 continuous crops of corn
followed by 2 1/2 to 3 years of Alfalfa and Rhodes grass pasture.1/ At full
development in year 6 the proposed rotation would yield 600 ha of corn (double
cropped) and 600 ha of pasture grown at any one time, with 400 ha being sowi
to new pasture, and 400 ha transferretd from old pasture to corn in each year.
The annual phasing of ploughing, land preparation, planting, harvesting, and
green fodder cutting is illustrated in chart 1.
Forag- Production
7.        Details of estimated crop yields and total annual dry matter produc-
tion are shown in Table 6. Total annual forage production at full development
1/   In full development-year six-total production of manure would be about
94,000 tons and would be used on the farm. There would be no stacking
in the feedlot and no disposal problem would be created. The use of
manure from the feedlot would economize with the application of fer-
tilizers and the quantity required for the production per ha would be
small and amount to about So.Sh. 185 (US$30) annually.



ANNEX 10
Page 3
in year 6, including green cut pasture, corn silage and corn grain, would
amount to 15,780 tons of dry matter (D.M.) or 13.2 tons of D.M. per productive
irrigated hectare - 1,200 ha. Forage would be converted to meat on the basis
of about 630 kg D.M. per animal over 75 days, producing an increment of about
70 kg liveweight, at a conversion ratio of 8:1. Growth rate and feed consump-
tion details are shown in Tables 6 and 7.
8.        Basic fodder for fattening animals would be corn silage.  It would
represent about 45% of the total feed requirement, be harvested at approxi-
mately 28 - 30% D.M., stored in open trench type silos and covered with plas-
tic sheeting. Additionally approximately 20% of the total feed would be annu-
ally grown and harvested as corn, mainly during the drier period of January
and February and it would be stored lightly crushed.
9.        In the first year of operation (Project year 2), half of the total
corn fodder grown (97,500 cattle feed days) would be withheld from feeding.
This would provide a permanent revolving feed reserve as an insurance against
crop failure, water shortage etc. All pasture forage would be harvested
green (in 7 to 10 cuts per annum), and fed directly to fattening cattle. It
might be necessary during the dry months in certain years to provide additional
supplementary feed in the form of hay, although in view of the feed reserve
this prospect is unlikely. Although the projections do not include hay feeding,
provision is made for one set of haymaking equipment.
10.       Operating costs could be reduced by the introduction of direct grazing
of pasture by fattening cattle, particularly during the first few weeks of
feeding. Because of possible management complications (cattle movement, ditch
damage) the projections have not allowed for this, but grazing would be tried
on a trial basis under the supervision of the Farm Manager. Electric fence
units would be provided so as to facilitate inexpensive control of grazing
cattle.
11.       Pasture forage projections are based on species presently grown in
Somalia. Higher producing varieties such as Napier and Guinea Grass would be
introduced. Under tropical conditions, irrigation, and heavy fertilizer appli-
cations, higher yields than those assumed are likely to be obtained.
Feedlot Operations
12.       At full production (year 6) 25,000 cattle would be fattened annually.
Approximately 80% of the animals (20,000) would come from the grazing ranches,
and 20% (5,000) would be selected from local markets. The finishing of ani-
mals in the feedlot would considerably increase the market value of meat. It
is this increase in grade, coupled with the liveweight gain of the animals
which would make the feedlot an economic proposition.



ANNEX 1 0
Page 4
13.       In the feedlot which would have an open air design similar to that
used at Lanet in Kenya, the daily ration of 8.8 kg D.M. would consist of 45%
maize silage, 20% maize grain, 32% green pasture, and 3% low protein oil seed
cake (sesame or cotton seed). Daily growth rates of about 0.90 kg liveweight
are assumed. As it has been estimated that cattle would have an average live-
weight of 300 kg on entering the feedlot, the total increase in weight per
animal should average about 70 kg over the 75 day period 1/. The weight estimates
are based on limited trials in Somalia, and commercial results obtained with
the same breed of cattle (many of which originate in Somalia) in Kenya. The
proposed ration contains a somewhat higher proportion of energy than would be
necessary under optimal conditions, but this assumption provides a further
technical contingency. Lowering the energy in the ration would increase the
annual throughput of cattle from any given area, or amount of forage produced,
thereby increasing income. When finished, the 370 kg animal would have at
least a dressing out percentage of 50% 2/. At full production incremental live-
weight gains of cattle in the feedlot would be about 23%, but the gain of
carcass weight would be over 30%. Total annual incremental carcass meat pro-
duced would be about 1,100 tons or 920 kg per productive or irrigated ha
(Tables 7 and 8).
Management and Labor
14.       A feedlot operation of the size and scale proposed would require
organizational and managerial skills not now available in Somalia and an
international firm of consultants would be employed to fill the posts re-
quired under the Project during development and to give on-the-job training
for local personnel. For the first four years, the Irrigated Fodder Farm
and Feedlot would be under the overall control of an expatriate Farm Manager,
who would be responsible for management and coordination of crop production,
farm machinery, and cattle feeding. For three and one half years he would
be supported by an internationally recruited irrigation specialist Master
Mechanic. Each of these would be responsible for the in-service training
of Somalis. Somali technicians would assume full operational management in
year five. Each would then have had at least one year as acting manager
under the appropriate specialist, and professional training at home and
abroad. Fully developed the farm would employ a total staff of about 250
(Table 4).
Marketing
15.       Finished cattle would be transported from the feedlot to the meat
factory in Kismayu, on the Kismayu - Gelib highway - a distance of approxi-
mately 80 km, by modern truck cattle transporters. Supply to the plant would
be spread evenly throughout the year, with approximately 90 head being avail-
able per killing day.
1/   Estimated that animals during the first five days in the feedlot would not
gain any weight and that during the remaining 70 days the daily weight
gain would be one kg.
2/   Results in Kenya show the dressing out percentage for the same class of
animal to be between 51 and 55 percent.



ANNEX 10
Page 5
Animal Health Control
16.       Animal health control would be the responsibility of a qualified,
resident Somali veterinarian, assisted by an animal health assistant who
would be a graduate of the Animal Health Training School at Mogadishu. Except
for those animals purchased directly from the markets (about 5,000) and for
which extensive veterinary and vaccine treatment would be provided, all cattle
would have spent one year under veterinary supervision and quarantine, prior
to entering the feedlot. Although the incidence of Tsetse fly and the accom-
panying Trypanosomiasis is known to be high in nearby bush and riverine areas,
because of the proximity of cleared land and banana plantations, incidence is
not serious on the proposed farm site. However to minimize incidence of
infection full provision has been made for the prophylactic treatment of all
cattle entering the feedlot. Details of treatments and vaccinations, together
with cost estimates, are shown in Table 4.

1/ It is estimated that the Project should be fully developed in five years
and therefore, all estimates, calculations and cash flows are based on
this. However, in this table alone a six-year period has been projected.
This allows for possible difficulties in Project management and organization

Page 1
SOMALIA
TRANS-JUBA LIVESTOCK PROJECT
Organization and Management
1.        Project activities would be coordinated by the Ministry of Livestock,
Forestry and Range (MLFR) and the Project would be executed by LDA and the
Veterinary Department of MLFR (Chart 1). LDA would be responsible for all
construction work and would operate markets, stockroutes, holding grounds,
and the production ranches. The Veterinary Department, which is now respon-
sible for all Government activities related to animal health except pe.rt
veterinary services would be responsible for disease control. The Project
would require some reorganization and strengthening of LDA. LDA's present
staff lacks the required expertise and experience, and Government, hqs agreed
that during development the management and organizatiorn of the Project should
be centered around key internationally recruited personnel.
Project Organization within LDA
2.        LDA is headed by a General Manager and has its headquarteis in Moga-
dishu; it operates through six regional offices throughout the col;atry, one
of which is in Kismayu, the administrative center of the Project area. LDA
now has three major departments: Marketing, Development, and Finance Adminis-
tration (Chart 2). While all departments need to be strengthened, it is essen-
tial for the Project that the Marketing Department be reorganized on a priority
basis and a new department, the Project Development and Training Unit, be
established under the direction of the Project Manager. The department would
supervise Project implementation, and would be phased out on Project completion.
3.        Hitherto LDA has been engaged predominantly in live cattle trading
for export or for the Kismayu meat factory. Its experience with production
related activities is limited and there is not sufficient evidence that it
could implement the proposed Project with its present staff and organ.zation.
During both implementation and at full development, Project organization
would be centered around six posts, to be established (Charts 3 and 4):
(a) Project Director, would be responsible to the LDA's General 2Ianager
for overall coordination of Project development, production, in-service local
staff training and consultancy. He would also be responsible, with the
assistance of the Project Finance Officer, for Project related procurement,
contracts, reimbursement claims and progress reports;



ANNEX 19
Page 2
(b) Senior Grazing Ranch Manager, would be responsible for the
establishment and initial operation of the two grazing ranches, including
the training of Somali ranch managers.  He would also conduct field trials
on range improvement;
(c) Irrigated Fodder Farm and Feedlot Manager, would be responsible
for the operation of the irrigated fodder farm including forage production,
feedlot operatron, use of machinery, as well as on the selection and in service
training of Somali technicians;
(d) irrigation Specialist, would be responsible for the irrigation opera-
tions on the irrigated fodder farm and would coordinate irrigation with feed
production.
(e) Project Finance Officer, would be primarily responsible for all
financial .spects of the Project including management of funds and procurement
and disburtement procedures, and would assist LDA's finance director in
strengthening the finance department; and
(f) Master Mechanic, would be responsible for the overall management and
repair of machinery, plant, and tractors including the establishment and
operation of the repair shop and spare parts section.
Terms of Reference for the six positions are in Appendix I.
4.       In view of the shortage of suitably trained and experienced Somali
staff candidates for these posts would be recruited internationally.  During
the first five years of Project operation these posts would be filled by such
staff, and local staff would receive professional and inservice training.
In year six, when Project construction and development would be completed
qualified and trained local staff would have taken over all posts (Chart 5).
Project Development and Training Unit (PDTU)
5.       The ?DTU would be headed by the Project Director who would be directly
responsible to LDA's General Manager. It would be located in Kismayu and
would be responsible for the survey, siting and construction of all development
work required by the Project. PDTU would direct the operations of the produc-
tio:i ranches through a senior ranch manager, irrigation specialist, irrigated
farm a.m feedlot manager and a master mechanic. The Project finance officer,
'ecated in M'ogadishu, would also be a member of this department. The Project
construction 3nd maintenance unit (PCMU) within PDTU would be established to
undertake all development work required by the marketing and disease control
components of the Project, excluding boreholes, which would be drilled and
equipped by contract with the water Development Agency (WDA) or by private
contractor. The PDTU, unless given other responsibility, would be abolished
after Project completion in year six. LDA's development department would
issume a continuous training function and the PCMU would eventually be trans-
formed into the maintenance unit responsible to the development department.
Before, or upon, disbanding of PDTU, a Production Department would be created
at LDA's headquarters which would supervise ranching and fattening operations



ANNEX 19
Page 3
set up under the Project as well as other production activities hitherto
managed by the development department (Chart 3).
Disease Control
6.       The Director of Animal Health and Production in the Veterinary
Depattment would be responsible for supervision of the veterinary staff of
the Project's disease control component.
Marketing Department
7.       The Marketing Department would have a market intelligence and promo-
tion service (MIPS) and trading service (TS). The Director would communicate
with the field units directly and through the two Service Chiefs. The MIPS
would collect, evaluate and disseminate market related statistical data, and
design and execute programs to improve LDA's marketing programs and procedures.
The TS would supervise the operation and maintenance of markets, stockroutes,
holding grounds and other marketing facilities and would supervise contracts,
regionally coordinate purchases and sales and liaise with the Production
Department.
The Kismayu Regional Department
8.       During Project implementation, the regional office would have a
development and maintenance section, a marketing section, a finance adminis-
tration section, and a port veterinary section. The production section would
be added at full Project development. The regional director would be in
direct communication with the head office, but flexibility would be introduced
so that the best working arrangements could be effected.
9.       The development and maintenance section would, at full development,
have a mobile staff and equipment unit (physically located at the irrigated
fattening farm) which would maintain all existing LDA facilities in the area.
Operating and maintenance personnel would be stationed at the holding grounds,
markets, marshalling yards, and stockroutes. The marketing section would
comprise the market teams in Badada, Hadweineh Busar, Lugh, Afmadu holding
ground for Tabda and Masketi, and Kismayu holding ground for Kismayu, Afmadu
and Gelib. This section would also include a regional market intelligence
coordinator and a regional market development coordinator. The production section
would oversee the grazing ranches and the irrigated fodder farm and feedlot.
10.      Staffing would proceed according to the progress of Project construc-
tion. It is important that some of the market development programs, in par-
ticular the bull-calves saving campaign, start at the earliest possible date
to assure the availability of feeder steer early in the Project. The PDTU,
although working in close cooperation with the Regional Director, would not
be subordinate to him.



ANNEX 19
Page 4
Staff Availability and Recruitment Procedures
11.      The Project envisages the employment of six qualified and experienced
professionals to be recruited through an international consulting firm. the
remaining posts would be filled by local recruitment. However, it would be
necessary to give substantial in service training to all locally recruited
staff. Government approved and established pay scales and conditions of serv-
ice already exist within the LDA for all levels of expertise and experience.
Junior staff are directly recruited at present, whereas management posts are
filled by advertisement and personal interview.
Training
12.      The Project provides about US$250,000 for diploma training and short
term study tours. Four graduates would receive training overseas and about
ten students would be given diploma training in countries with similar eco-
logical conditions; it is assumed that about half of the students would become
directly involved in Project implementation, and half would be for the MLFR
and the Kismayu meat factory. Candidates would be selected by LDA in conjunc-
tion with the MLFR. Training would be in animal production, range management,
irrigated feed production, marketing, machinery utilization and maintenance
or in similar disciplines which would qualify the trainees for subsequent
employment within the Project, or within MLFR. Two Somali technicians or
supervisors from the Kismayu meat factory would be sent under the training
program to plants with similar produce in order to familiarize them with the
operating procedures and measures required to meet international standards of
hygiene. Short term study tours would be proposed by the Project Director and
approved by LDA in consultation with IDA.



ANNEX 19
Appendix 1
Page 1
SOMALIA
TRANS-JUBA LIVESTOCK PROJECT
Draft Terms of Reference, Duties, Responsibilities and
Qualifications for LDA Project Staff
1.  Project Director
The Project Director would be directly responsible to the General
Manager of LDA for Project management and organization.
Duties and Responsibilities
The Project Director would be required for four years, and would
have, inter alia, the following specific duties and responsibilities, which
he could delegate to the technical staff of the Project Organization:
(a) manage, execute and evaluate the Project in accordance
with policies and procedures of the borrower;
(b) prepare with the assistance of his staff, the development
programs and projections for each Project related component. In
particular he would determine to what extent proposed mechanization
could be replaced by labor, without jeopardizing the fodder production
required for the feedlot;
(c) provide the supervision and technical assistance necessary
to ensure the successful start-up and completion of each development
component of the Project;
(d) advise the General Manager of LDA on the selection and recruitment
of local technical staff required for Project implementation;
(e) coordinate and supervise the duties and responsibilities of Project
related staff;
(f) advise the General Manager of LDA on, and assist with the selection
of consultants required by the Project from time to time;
(g) establish and supervise the maintenance of suitable records and
accounts for all development components so as to facilitate Project evaluation;



ANNEX 19
Appendix 1
Page 2
(h)  prepare quarterly and annual progress reports for submission to LDA
for transmittal to IDA;
(i) advise LDA on, and assist with, the procurement of all materials,
equipment, machinery, and other requisites necessary for Project implementation;
(j) prepare specifications for, and supervise the selection of tenders
where International or Local Competitive Bidding is considered necessary for
the supply of materials or machinery, and/or construction works.
Qualifications
The Project Director should have the following minimum qualifica-
tions:
(a) a university degree in Agricultural Science, Agricultural Economics,
Agricultural Commerce, Business Administration, or its equivalent;
(b) a broad professional experience of at least 10 years, preferably
in Agriculture or related sectors in countries with similar ecological
conditions;
(c) a proven ability in organization and management, and experience
with staff management, preferably in a similar country.
2.   Senior Grazing Ranch Manager
The Senior Grazing Ranch Manager would be required for three years,
would be directly under the Project Director, and would be responsible inter
alia for the construction and start up production of the two grazing ranches.
Duties and Responsibilities
(a) In consultation with the Project Director, select the final sites
for, and direct the establishment of the proposed two grazing ranches;
(b) supervise the operation and management of the two grazing ranches,
anl in consultation with the Project Director, and the Irrigated Fodder Farm
and Feedlot Manager coordinate and facilitate the timely supply of cattle to
the feedlot;
(c) devise a program of in-service training of the Somali ranch managers;
(d) devise and implement, a program of applied research of techniques of
range improvement;



ANNEX 19
Appendix 1
Page 3
(e) study and record the management, husbandry and cattle production
related customs among the nomadic cattle owners in the Lower Juba region;
(f) provide annually to the Project Director a written report on the
performance of the two ranches, and progress of investigations prescribed in 1|
(d) and (e) above;
(h) in the third year of Project implementation prepare after due consul-
tation with the Project Director a draft plan for the further development of
the cattle industry, in the Trans-Juba area based on the experience gained by
the Project.
Qualifications
The Senior Grazing Ranch Manager should have the following qualifi-
cations:
(a) a university degree in agricultural science, or its equivalent, with
a bias in animal husbandry and/or tropical agronomy;
(b) at least 3 years experience in ranch management, in low rainfall,
semi-arid tropical climate areas in the pasture animal complex as it relates
to rangeland management, as well as in all facets of cattle husbandry, manage-
ment, and breeding.
3.   Irrigation Specialist
The Irrigation Specialist would be required for about 3 years and
would be responsible to the Project Director for providing irrigation on the
irrigated fodder farm and feedlot.
Duties and Responsibilities
The Irrigation Specialist would be required to manage the irrigation
operations of the irrigated fodder farm. In carrying out these duties the
irrigation specialist would be under the direct supervision of the irrigated
fodder farm and feedlot manager and would inter alia.
(a) prepare a schedule of irrigation operations in accordance with
crop and soil requirement. The schedule should take into consideration
water holding capacities of the soils, frequencies of irrigations, timing
in relation to crop production, and possible changes in water quality at
different periods during the year;
(b) supervise operations and maintenance of the pumping plant;



ANNEX 19
Appendix 1
Page 4
(c) organize and oversee the work of the irrigator crews so that irriga-
tion water would be applied in proper quantities and according to schedule;
(d) coordinate irrigation operations with other activities on the farm;
(e) schedule the maintenance operations;
(f) provide for in-service training for the Somali irrigation specialist,
the irrigators, and the pumping plant employees;
(g) prepare, necessary activity reports.
Qualifications
The irrigation specialist should have the following minimum qualifi-
cations:
(a) a university degree in Agriculture with an Irrigation Option or
equivalent;
(b) five years experience in managing irrigation operations growing simi-
lar crops in an environment similar to that in Somalia.
4.   Irrigated Fodder Farm and Feedlot Manager
The Manager would be required for four years and would be directly
responsible to the Project Director in managing the production of feed
required for the feedlot.
Duties and Responsibilities
The Farm Manager would be responsible to the Project Director for the
start up and implementation of the irrigated fattening farm and feedlot opera-
tion. He would be responsible for overall management and coordination includ-
ing feed production and conservation, feedlot management, and machinery repair
and organization. He would inter alia:
(a) assist the Project Manager in the detailed planning and construction
of the irrigated fodder farm and feedlot;
(b) advise the Project Director on the employment of technical and other
staff needed for the farm;
(c) direct and coordinate the work of the Irrigation Specialist and the
Master Mechanic;



ANNEX 19
Appendix 1
Page 5
(e) direct the employment of skilled and unskilled staff required for
Project implementation;
(f) devise and implement in cooperation with the Veterinary Department
of the Ministry of Livestock Forestry and Range and resident Somali veteri-
narian a program of animal health control within the irrigated fodder farm
and feedlot;
(g) arrange for the timely supply of cattle for the feedlot with the
grazing ranches managers;
(h) coordinate, through the Project Director, the timely supply of
finished cattle to the Kismayu meat factory; and
(i) supervise adaptive research with various tropical forage crops, grass
and legume species.
qualifications
The Irrigated Fodder Farm and Feedlot Manager should have the follow-
ing qualifications:
(a) A university degree, diploma, or equivalent experience in irrigated
crop production, and animal husbandry and/or nutrition;
(b) experience with tropical crop and beef production;
(c) experience with the feedlot concept of beef feeding and management;
(d) a demonstrated organizational and managerial ability; and
(e) a demonstrated ability to handle and organize labor preferably in
areas of similar production conditions as the Trans-Juba area.
5.   Master Mechanic
The Master Mechanic would be required for three and a half years and
would be responsible to the Irrigated Fodder Farm and Feedlot Manager for the
overall organization and maintenance of machinery and equipment on the irri-
gated fodder farm and feedlot.
Duties and Responsibilities
The Master Mechanic would be responsible to the Irrigated Fodder
Farm and Feedlot Manager for the overall mechanical management, maintenance
and repair of machinery, tractors and pumping station including the supervision
and training of Somali operators and mechanics. He would, inter alia, have the
following duties and responsibilities:



ANNEX 19
Appendix 1
Page 6
(a) assist and advise the Farm Manager on all operations relating to all
Project related agricultural machinery and equipment and participate in the
selection of mechanics and machine operators needed for the Project;
(b) advise the Farm Manager on the procurement, type, operation, and
management of machinery, in order to execute the proposed feed production pro-
gram;
(c) establish and supervise the operation of the machinery repair shop,
including a properly organized spare parts section with a system for sustain-
ing the inventory;
(d) devise and supervise in consultation with the Farm Manager an adequate
training program for Somali mechanics. Tractor drivers, and machine operators;
Qualifications
The Master Mechanic should have the following minimum qualifications
and experience:
(a) a qualified "A" grade mechanics certificate, or its equivalent;
(b) a minimuam of 8 years experience in the repair and maintenance of
agricultural machinery;
(c) wide experience in the operation and management of agricultural
machinery, particularly that related to forage crop production and conserva-
tion; and
(d) a demonstrated ability to manage labor and train technical staff,
preferably in a similar environment as that in the Project area.
6.   Project Finance Officer
The Project Finance Officer would be directly responsible to the
Project Director for all financial matters relating to the Project. He is
also expected to act in an advisory capacity to LDA's Finance Director.
Duties and Responsibilities:
The Project Finance Officer would be required for four years and
would have the following duties and responsibilities:
(a) design an accounting and management information system suitable for
the Project;



ANNEX 19
Appendix 1
Page 7
(b) advise the Project Director on the appointment of clerical staff;
(c) prepare the necessary forms and records for the proper implementation
of the system;
(d) issue the necessary directives and instructions to personnel in the
field;
(e) train and supervise the account staff into the use of forms and
entering of data into the accounting records;
(f) supervise the preparation of periodical management information data
and financial statements by the Projects accounts staff;
(g) control during Project's development period procurement and disburse-
ment procedures and statements and progress reports as required by IDA; and
(h) advise LDA's Finance Director to strengthen LDA's Finance and Admin-
istration Department to take over the Project's accounts section at the end
of year four when the Project Finance Officers' employment ends.
Qualification and Experience:
The Project Finance Officer should have the following minimum
qualifications:
(a) a university degree and/or professional qualification in Accounting
and related subjects;
(b) a minimum of 5 years experience as chief accountant or controller;
(c) previous experience in designing and implementing of accounting
systems desirable.
