TRANS-JUBA LIVESTOCK PROJECT
Financial Analysis
1.        Separate rates of return have been calculated for the marketing and
production components of the Project in order to test their individual finan-
cial viability. Overhead charges have been distributed between the two com-
ponents in proportion to the amount of their respective capital costs. The
financial rate of return to the Project as a whole reflects LDA operations
only and does not take into account costs of So.Sh.4.4 million (US$700,000) for
feasibility studies, animal disease control and some training not directly
Project-related.
Marketing
2.        Costs of the marketing components consist of:
(a) capital and operating expenses for the stockroutes, the
markets, the market teams and the Afmadu holding ground;
(b) capital cost of the Project Construction and Maintenance
Unit (PCMU), and PCMU's operating costs including replace-
ment costs adjusted for a maintenance charge accounted for
by the grazing ranches and the irrigated fodder farm and
feedlot (Table 1, footnote 2);
(c) an overhead charge representing 22 percent of the cost for
the Project Development Unit and of Project-related training
that is directly related to the marketing component; and
(d) incremental livestock purchases.
3.        Financial benefits consist of sales of canning cattle to the meat
factory, immatures to the grazing ranches, selected feeders to the feedlot,
and exports of live cattle. Weights and prices are shown in Annex 5, Table 6.
4.        The financial rate of return for the marketing component (Table 1)
is 16 percent. Sensitivity analysis indicates that an increase in cattle
buying prices of 10 percent would reduce the rate of return to 2 percent
which indicates the component's sensitivity on prices. To maintain reasonable
prices and margins for all participants in the Project - nomadic producers,
LDA and t.he Kismayu meat factory - Government would be required that it would
consult annually with IDA on its cattle and beef pricing policies with the
objective of ensuring that such policies would include the encouragement of
higher offtake and an equitable distribution of benefits to all participants
in the beef cattle industry.



ANNEX 21
Page 2
Production
5.        The costs of the Project's production component consist of capital,
operating and replacement costs of the two grazing ranches, the irrigation
segment and the irrigated fodder farm and feedlot; overhead costs including
78 percent of the costs of the Project Development Unit and of Project-related
training directly related to production; and the cost of cattle purchases.
Financial benefits represent LDA's gross revenue from the operation of the
ranches and the fattening farm. Purchase and selling weights and prices of
fattened cattle are shown in Annex 5, Table 6.
6.        The financial rate of return of the production component (Table 2)
is 13%. Sensitivity analysis indicates that with a 10% increase in capital costs,
the return would be 12%; an increase of 10% in operating costs would give a
return of 10%. Furthermore, the rate of return would decrease to 10% if
cattle purchase prices increased by 10% and to 7% if cattle selling prices
fell by 10%.
Complete Project
7.        The above components of marketing and production would have an
estimated financial rate of return of 14% (Table 3). The rate of return
would decline to 10% in the event of a 10% rise in prices paid to cattle
producers, and to 6% if selling prices fell by 10%.
October 10, 1973

ANNEX 24
Page 1
SOMALIA
TRANS-JITBA LIVESTOCK PROJECT
Balance of Payments Impact
1.        Total foreign exchange cost, including So.Sh.8.8 million for con-
tingencies of the Project,is estimated at about So.Sh.45.6 million (US$7.3
million) which represents about 64 percent of total Project cost. Approx-
imately 90 percent of the foreign exchange would be required for capital
expenditures, training and feasibility studies and 10 percent for the ini-
tial three-years operating expenditures. At full development, the Project
would require annual imports, largely fuels and replacements of capital
items, valued at So.Sh.4.3 million and would earn foreign exchange from live
cattle exports of about So.Sh4.5 million annually. It would thus directly
produce a small foreign exchange surplus of So.Sh.0.2 million.
2.        Due to increased slaughter resulting from the Project, the Kismayu
meat factorv would require additional investments with a foreign exchange com-
ponent of approximately So. Sh. 4 million (US$640,000). At full Project
development, the factorv's incremental operating expenditures would have a
foreign exchange component of So. Sh. 12.8 million, while the factory's in-
cremental foreign exchange earnings would amount to So. Sh.36.8 million. The
meat factory, therefore, would have annual incremental net earnings of foreign
exchange of So. Sh.24 million (TTSq3.9 million).
3.        The IDA credit would be for US$10.0 million disbursed over a 5-year
period. Taking into account debt service payments on the IDA credit, the
overall effect to the economy would he a net inflow of foreign exchange of
So.Sh.51.6 million during the first five Project years. At full develop-
ment -from year six onwards - the annual foreign exchange surplus would be
So.Sh.23.7 million (US$3.8 million) during the grace period of the IDA
Credit, and So.Sh,23.3 million for years 11 to 20 (TUS$3.7 million). There
would be only a small foreign exchange deficit in year one of Project De-
velopment but none thereafter.
December 14, 1973

ANNEX 25
Page 1
SOMALIA
TRANS-JUBA LIVESTOCK PROJECT
Economic Analysis
1.        The financial calculations for LDA (Annex 21) were adjusted as
follows:
Additions to Benefits
(a) Taxes and duties paid by LDA on inputs have been deducted;
(b) export duties on live cattle and municipal taxes, have been
added back to the Project's incremental income;
(c) the financial cost of LDA's cattle purchases from nomads
has been adjusted downward for the following reasons. LDA's
purchases under the Project would include increased offtake
from the traditional herd which would not have occurred
without the Project. In addition LDA's purchases would
include animals which, but for the Project, would have been
sold to other buyers in Somalia and Kenya. It is only this
second category of existing sales foregone which is charged in
the economic calculation. Thus of the 53,000 head purchased
annually at full development by LDA only 35,000, representing
existing sales foregone, are charged in the economic analysis;
(d) unskilled and semi-skilled labor employed under the Project's
marketing component and in the Project Construction and
itaintenance Unit is assumed not to be fully employable out-
side the Project and has been shadow priced at 50% of market
wages after income taxes. Unskilled labor employed on the
two grazing ranches and the irrigated fodder farm and feedlot
has been assumed to have alternative employment opportunities
at market wage rates on the nearly banana plantations and has
not been shadow priced. Skilled labor is in short supply and
its cost has not been adjusted; and
(e)  overall as a result of the Project mortality would be reduced
from 1.5% to one percent and weight losses from about 13% to
7%.  Some of these benefits would be enjoyed by LDA and are
reflected in its financial return. However, the majority of
the benefits would accrue to the nomadic producers and accordingly
this has been added as an additional benefit to the economy.



ANNEX 25
Page 2
Additions to Costs
(a) It is estimated that ranchland occupied by the grazing ranches
and the Afmadu holding ground currently produces a marketable
surplus of 1,570 head of cattle annually valued at So. Sh.
433,000. The annual benefits derived from the Project have
thus been reduced by this amount. Current production on the
proposed site of the irrigated fodder farm and feedlot is
insignificant;
(b) the Disease Control Component and non-Project related
training costs are not LDA operations and were not included
in its financial analysis. They have however been treated
as a cost to the economy.
2.        The amount of So. Sh. 2 million for feasibility studies has not
been included as a cost either in the Financial or the Economic Analysis,
and no shadow prices for foreign exchange was considered necessary for this
analysis.
3.        With these adjustments, summarized in Table 1, the rate of return
is estimated at 25%. A 10% increase in cost inputs would lower the return
to 20% and a decrease in selling prices to 20%.
4.        The Project would also improve utilization of previous investments
in the Project area and East of the Juba River. Further benefits to the
economy in the form of value added during processing at the Kismayu meat
factory are not taken into account in the above calculation; both costs and
benefits of the factory are excluded.
Economic Analysis of Individual Components
5.01      In calculating the individual rates of return of separate Project
components, the two grazing ranches and the irrigated fodder farm and feedlot
are strongly interdependent and could not be treated as separate components.
The preparatory process on the grazing ranches for subsequent intensive
fattening is the first phase of an integrated cattle fattening operation
that also includes necessary animal health precautionary measures. From
a Project analysis point of view it seems appropriate, for the stated reasons,
to treat marketing (of immatures and fattened animals) grazing ranches and
fattening as three operations or phases of one and the same production
process. Profitability of individual operations would be incidental to the
distribution of costs and benefits among the various phases of the production
process i.e. from the point of purchase of immatures to the delivery of
fattened cattle at the factory gate.  Considering the above and adjusting
the financial calculation of the two grazing ranches and irrigated fodder
farm and feedlot, the rate of return to the economy of this integrated pro-
duction component is 21%. The rate of return of LDA's cattle marketing
component is about 39%.
